Cereus & Limnic: Escape From Okinawa \- Type B

*For my brothers, always the video boyz*

**Recovered by the International Genetic Ethics Tribunal (IGET): March 19, 2037**  
The following field archive was assembled from fragmented transmissions, field logs, and biological samples obtained during the Okinawa Containment Event of 2026\.  
Sections of this record are incomplete, corrupted, or irreconcilable with accounts previously declassified by the United States Space Force Department of Otherworldly Affairs (USSFDOA).  
IGET acknowledges the presence of multiple, contradictory narratives surrounding these events. The authenticity of this version cannot be independently verified.  
Read with caution. Contents may disturb.

# Prologue

## [Archivist’s Note](https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/1HDS4p5nXX6bqnTdXx5beemVPnprqIaAEOvDNx6ZEvDM/edit)

I knew before I saw the signature that this would be his final act. At the foot of a witness statement, the 4-letter mark was there. His tiny scribbled name, discreet, unflourished. A reminder that dad had held this paper, sat over it, thought about it. This made my spine curve like a withering tree branch.

This damned record— a sandy pit with an antlion lying in ambush— is what he died for? By viewing its contents, I'd already begun the slow slide down. The greedy fine grains shifted beneath me. I was in the insectary now, watching the snap-limbed bugs writhe through glass, dismemberment on full display. At first I couldn't believe I'd have to stick my hands in there. I stared for a while, arms at my sides, just feeling my pulse flexing at my neck with black-night highway speed worry.

But after a team of suits deposited two small truckloads of files into my study, I knew. The musty moats of dust swirled like flies over a cadaver. Everything smelled aged and alien. It had been there. In Okinawa. That dank island air was corrosive perfume, inhabiting anything that stayed within its perimeter for too long.

Years of fighting bureaucracy and it was finally here.

Yet, even after running my finger over the top box’s beaten sides, it still felt far away. The sun-scratched U.S. Space Force insignia, peeled chain of custody stickers, fingertip sweat smears— so many hands had held the boxes without understanding why they existed at all.

If I ever step foot on those shores, I would know it by the lingering scent of storied decay floating everywhere. I'm starting to understand why Dad never talked about it.

Secret. That's how his feelings were classified. One stabbing pump of my heart forced me to recall how avoidant he always was.

After I'd signed several release forms, the team of dark-suited deliverymen began filing out. The man in charge lingered, looking up to me (under average height), pores oozing greasy sleep debt on his face. He glanced at the boxes, then back at me.

"You sure you want to handle this alone, ma'am?" His neutral American accented voice carried concerned warning. "We can leave a team to help catalog—"

"I'm sure." I slipped my pen over the last form’s signature line. No direct look at him.

"It's just... your father's estate specifically requested you. By name. Most family members, they'd take one look at a haul like this and—"

"I'm not most family members. I'm the only family member… for now."

"No ma'am, I suppose not." His expression turned gloomy, resigned. He blew his peanut butter sandwich breath out hard then said, "Well I'm just sayin’, for nearly half a century, these files have occupied a single room in some hardly acknowledged U.S. military storage facility. Few ventured in there, and when they did, they signed their log, then quick-stepped out. That's what I heard. It's like they’re cancer-causing or cursed, or both."

"I understand."

He looked as if he wanted to say more. Or to try and protect me, this small tired Asian woman, from the creatures in the boxes. He had the eyes of a half-time hero.

"I'll be going then. Sorry for your loss and good luck with it."

"Thank you."

I shut the door. Then heard a slide by the wall. The wall of cartons was a portrait with traveling eyes.  
Or was it something else? I suspected so. It wasn't only the droopy sight of the boxes that spurt liquid dread through me. The way they slanted— exhausted, defeated— triggered micro-shifting groans from their lids. Not every few minutes. Constantly. A low chorus of cardboard stress and settling weight, like joints popping in an empty house. I tried to tell myself it was normal. Boxes settle. But boxes don't breathe.

Whatever language spoke through those disembodied creases stayed indecipherable— but made me whip my head up in watchful paranoia. Every few minutes. Every \*tap\*. Every \*crack\*. Sometimes when I was certain I'd been facing the window, I'd turn to find a box had shifted orientation. Corners now pointed at my desk. Lids slightly more ajar.

As much as I didn't want to admit it, staring at the shoulder-high wall of sagging containers, there was a force emanating from them. Not metaphorical— physical. It steamed out subtly at first, like exhaust over asphalt on a balmy summer afternoon. Then it intensified. The air near the boxes had weight. As if the space itself had been compressed and now wanted to expand into my lungs. I breathed shallowly. Despite the climatized blow of air pushing wisps of my hair under the vents, the side of my body warmed when I passed by.

My back flexed and arms shook when I lifted the first one down and pulled out the first file— a folder goblin with papers hanging out of the folds, like an overstuffed laundry hamper reeking and half-dry.  
When I lifted the lid fully, the smell hit first— not mildew, though that was there. Other smells puffed out. Copper, salt and another hard to name. Whatever it was made my hindbrain scream *meat* even though these were only papers. 

The first file folder was damp to the touch, defying the room’s refrigerator dry cold. My fingertips came away clean, but I rubbed them together anyway, expecting residue that wasn't there.  
Pulse jamming in my ears, I cleared my throat and sat. Adjusted my jacket. Sip of water— ritual, not thirst. I smoothed the first folder flat with both palms. Once. Twice. Three times. Only then could I read it.

As I pulled the first document free, I noticed something I hadn't seen in the initial inspection. In the margin of a transmission log, someone had written in pencil: \*They're listening. Even now.\* The handwriting wasn't my father's.

I didn't know yet if that made it better or worse. But I was ready.

Prepared to spend as long as necessary digging through fear and falsity, to learn what my father had become in that place.

To discover what followed him home and killed him.

## Arrested Silence (Higa Bonta \- 比嘉ボンタ)

I find myself marooned on a corpse-island. Egad\! Even bugs are acting up. Even the flies balled into clusters behind me (kinda like floating mochi) as I sat fishing early this morning. The port was a phone with no notifications— sound was muted but 変な things crept at my back.

On the walk down things were odd. The tapping of my house key against my small water flask connected to my belt clanked dull as an old bell with each step. That got my ear for a block. First it was the sky: how the clouds oozed rusty dawn in an inverted pyramid— that was the first bad sign.

Then it was the noise. Why bring it up? Since there was scant to be heard, is why. While Awase Port can be quiet, this was a mean silence.  The type that comes before an *ambuscade* (finally got to use this word\!) like in Fortnight and an enemy dancing, or tea-bagging over your grave. And the sounds I did hear were awkward, no cap: vending-truck jingles cut mid-note— not trailing off, but *erased*, like someone  muted it, a sawtoothing scooter clapped then ripped at a corner, obaa’s antique Enka radio coughed out an intro to a song I didn’t know, then went to radio snow. I didn’t know ears could strain like eyes do trying to hear. This must be what it’s like to get old (I’ve heard all the wax builds up). It’s like all sound was password protected and one by one, the owners forgot the secret code. Everybody got locked out. (Strange\!)

I reached my spot and soundlessness came back. Funny quiet. マジで nothing stirred. Frozen were trees. Frozen were cicadas. Frozen were waves. Water like a clean window after wiping, spotless— impossiblly clean. Time took a break this morning, only resuming work as I sat with my rod out in the water. Took me a while to breathe normally after seeing that.

Mom had told me not to come out today. And said if I did to take my sister with me. But I couldn’t be bothered. She takes crikey forever ほんとぅぅに for-e-ver to get up and get ready. When I set out at 0530, I heard her light snores and smelled her さば-stink sleep from her futon. By the time I got back, in an hour, she’d still be sleep. Likely.

Forty-five minutes passed. Nothing bit. My rod stayed useless, a toy floating the tide. Real lame\! So I packed up to head home. At this point, the happy blue of the sky was well over the sad hue of the ocean. That damnable heat made my forehead shimmer.

On the way back, the lack of normal *acustics* made me walk faster. So I did. Outside the 7/11, two elder whispered. "Ships," one mumbled. "Foreign ships westward near Itoman." 

I heard something about *beasts* walking ashore— half-men, half-shadows like Moreau’s own vivisected creatures. Was this a joke? The other murmured, "heard the ships were empty. Coffins without crew." 

ヤバイ\! Who can say? Mom calls it *gajimaru* gossip. Says it's harmless, but still keeps her distance from those old guys. "They talk too much nonsense." She said that with worry last week. I dreamed of ghosts after that. Ones ferrying me off to far away shores, to a place I shouldn’t, yet needed to be. Wouldn’t that be something\!

Trudging home I looked up. The clouds— still in that inverted pyramid. Still pointing west. Yomitan? Chatan?

The shape hadn't moved in over an hour or two.

Clouds don't do that, and cities make sounds.

I went back to my ebook and ate a bento lunch. Meio is still sleep.

## Brothers in the Battlefield 

To let go of lagom is to see a red arm half-attached; it clings to God’s law of bloodletting.

That idea came to me after another body smashed into the sand facedown. Mouth open, hard sand dried his tongue further. Another death at my hand.

"Bro\! You gonna paint a picture\!? Get yo ass over here and get low\!"

I ducked and dashed over to join my younger brother. Under the beating sun, Scorch’s face was blacker than back home; his scraggled low beard had specks of coral in it. Spit came out when he said, "Good, good nigga you made it. Was worried a little there."

The two of us hunched behind a portable barricade. Another bomb, two, then three exploded across the beach. From where I knelt, this Siege Smart Shield appeared thin. The pinging of enemy fire on the other side made me wonder if it would maintain integrity.

Between heavy breaths I said, "You didn't think I'd make it?"

"Of course yo bigorexia black ass was gonna make it. Im more concerned about that shooter in the brush and if Sun can keep up. All dat pilates he do ain't no good here."

A surge of scared made my arm tingle. I shook it to return the feeling. The grip of my rifle grounded me.

"It’s good for balance and strength."

He rolled his eyes. "Pot shot bonus. Gimme the weights any day."

"Last weight you saw was in line at the \[REDACTED\] last chow."

"Ha. Ha. You ain’t funny."

I signaled quiet, surprised it actually worked for once. From the staticky radio came Sun’s voice.

"Y’all read?"

"I do," I said. I glanced at Scorch. He had been examining his rifle like an art piece. Then had moved on to staring at the unkept greenery to our right on the beach, no doubt envisioning how he would make it more orderly— control it. Or he was attempting to place the enemy unit that had been haunting us since we’d heard the first order. I gave him a shove.

"What\!?" he jolted.

I made three sharp taps at my ear. His annoyance took 40 seconds longer than I liked to fade, but he grudged stiffness, he switched his radio on and pressed his earpiece deeper into his thick skull.

"Oh, go ahead. We’re good. Go Sun."

"Unit completed recon up \_\_\_."

"Nigga where you at?" Scorch duck-walked closer. Two more pings of the barrier sounded just as he stopped. He dropped his head lower at the same time that I did.

"I’m up the beach on the sea side, dug in. They won’t find me."

"Cool. What do you see?"

"Enemy has approximately 5 units, and two shooters are on the jungle side."

"We knew that already. Son-of-a-bitch spot was pinned out here. Did you find out anything ACTUALLY useful?"

I punched Scorch in the arm and shook my head. He returned a flash of mocking laughter.

"Put your see-through lenses on," Sun sounded as if he were gritting his teeth. I knew he was holding back unleashing a scalding return to our youngest brother.

We hurried to put the gear on, intact and unzipping them. They still had that sleep-preserved smell of simplicity, manufactured item.  
"These things never work," I complained.

"Biggest waste of fuckin’ coin on these pieces," Scorch.

\[BEGIN BATTLE SEQUENCE\]

\[DRONE SHOT – Overhead Recon Feed\]  
The beach stretched out like a scar—cratered sand, smoke curling into broken-finger columns, the surf dragging bodies and wreckage back and forth. Wind gusted hard enough to scatter grit into the haze. Sunlight filtered pale through scattered clouds. Fuel reeked on the air, sharp enough to sting. The view was not serene.

\[SHADE’S ACCOUNT\]  
The next barrage took down our cover. The Smart Shield cracked, hummed, and collapsed with a pathetic whine. I spat grit, muttered, "Piece of shit government dollars."

"Don’t matter now," Scorch barked, already lifting his rifle. "Time to go hot." His shot hit clean through the brush. The wet slap echoed back. "Haircut."

Another explosion rattled the barricade. The beach opened on us.

\[SUN – Audio Overlay\]  
"You’re Overexposed. Don’t Dare a Dare." His voice snapped through the comms, clipped and tight.

Scorch cursed, "Just ’cause you get Overexposed fast don’t mean I do\!"

"Shut the fuck up. Eyes forward," I cut in, sliding beside him.

"Yeah, yeah," he grumbled, "you don’t gotta tell me twice."

\[DRONE SHOT – Low Sweep\]  
Enemy fire streaked across the dunes. Two signatures pinned their sights on our coordinates.

\[SHADE’S ACCOUNT\]  
Scorch wiped grit from his cheek where a round had grazed him. He growled, "Fuck that—I’m Daring."

He slammed his rifle into mine, magnetic coupler sparking. Shared mag locked. The barrels flared white-hot, muzzle flash stretching longer than it had any right to. Heat shimmered between us. Dare Called: Overheat Volley. His Energy spiked, his Exposure screamed red across the HUD.

I cursed, blurred into Ghost Step, my body jerking sharp as the Dare drained me. Enemy fire tracked instantly. We rolled and spat rounds back. It was not a graceful maneuver.

Scorch’s weapon jammed. Enemy scope found him in that instant. I dove, smashed him sideways into the sand.

"That would’ve been a D-rank death," I snapped.

He coughed grit, then smirked bitter: "Ranking don’t matter if I’m already smoking."

We split, trying to flank. I circled left, he pushed right. Gunfire shredded the brush. The jungle seethed like it was alive. The plan was not flawless.

\[DRONE SHOT – New Contact Detected\]  
A mechanical shriek cut through the wind. Hover-signature appeared: air bike, fifteen feet off the ground, rider armored, weapon mounted.

\[SHADE’S ACCOUNT\]  
"This ain’t what I signed up for\!" I shouted.

Scorch barked back, "Aim for the fans\! Drop the bastard\!" He fired, but the rounds pinged harmless off reinforced plating. The rider strafed us with heavier fire, pinning us into the sand.

\[SUN – Entry Recorded\]  
Sun broke cover at last, calm and steady. Dare Called: Precision Command. His Energy burned steady, but already his Exposure climbed fast. His voice cut the chaos into lanes. "On me. Triangle."

We locked in. Scorch blazing, me darting in, Sun calling strikes. The rider tore the surf into blood-mist spray.

\[DRONE SHOT – Mid-Engagement\]  
Exposure meters spiked in tandem. Shade \+ Scorch attempted Dual Dare Sync. Rounds staggered in rhythm. The outcome was not guaranteed. Sync slipped. Dare failed.

\[SHADE’S ACCOUNT\]  
The rider pressed the advantage, shredding the sand in lines.

Then Sun’s voice: "Together. Now."

We didn’t hesitate. Triple Dare Called: Daybreak Trinity. Weapons locked in sequence. Energy surged, Exposure maxed, HUD glitching like the world itself cracked. Time stuttered. The air warped.

All three volleys converged. The air bike ripped apart mid-scream, the rider crumpling into smoke.

\[DRONE SHOT – Closing Image\]  
Three heat signatures stood amid smoke and ruin, panting, Overexposed, half-spent.

\[SHADE’S ACCOUNT\]  
The treeline shifted though the wind had stopped. I spat grit, shook my head. "This fight ain’t over."

\[AFTER THE BATTLE SEQUENCE AND THE BROTHERS ARE REUNITED\]

Alone a single tree multiplies, stretches, and shrinks to police line up heights. Together they form a barrier of tropical terror. 

"Damn." Scorch stared forestward.

"What’s over there?" I joined him while, behind me, Sun was hot-focused on his tablet, probably mapping out our next moves.

"You don’t feel that?"

"Feel what?"

"Wind’s pickin’ up. Came outta nowhere. And somethin’ back there don’t seem too happy about what we did."

Late afternoon brightness was buried behind clouds, yet even with less visibility I saw what he was talking about. Sensed it too.

Where the beach met the brambled bush, the jungle swung to some unseen rhythm, first with the low whip of branches, then with the prick of dried stickiness along my neck, then with the dank reek of the woods carried seaward into the corpse-chomping surf, until even the thin trunks— unarmleted, stripped bare— bent as though they might break under the sudden invisible push.

"Don’t You Feel That?" I heard Scorch say again.

"Feel What?"

"Wind Picked Up."

"Foo you just said that. What the fuck." I shot back.

"Shade, you say somethin’?" Scorch gave me a rare genuinely curious look.

"I said ‘what the fuck’."

"Oh oh, I thought I heard your voice from over there." He gestured at the treeline where the enemy had been. A choral formation of trees muttering some song of worship to ungodly union. They stared right at us. I didn’t want to look at it anymore.

"Scorch… c’mon." I tugged at his arm, but he kept seeing over there.

"Shade?"

"Yeah?"

"Dis place is fucked. We gotta get off. Go home." He thumbed the chain on his chest. Signal of vow he acknowledged too late. That’s all I knew.

"We will. We’re gonna do it bro."

"If they let us."

He never took his eyes off the trees. 

## Archivist's Dictated Note: Review of field footage obtained from a helmet-mounter camera (presumably Shade’s)

Present the grainy picture are Scorch and Shade. Both are seated on their loaded packs. Two black in color lumps, hardly visible due to the time of day. Dusk is dimming in the sky. Trees rustling their schff-schff sound and small waves coming the shore are audible in front of their small fire.

Begin review at 2356:

"Hey nigga, did you see me get fat one in the brush?"

"Yeah."

"Why you sound so, so unenthused?"

"Huh? I was? My bad, didn't mean to."

"Man NVM. See this why I don't be sayin’ nuthin’ to you ass."

"Because why? You celebratin’ a kill like damn Afghan terrorist?"

"See\! See\! My nigga why. So. Serious?"

\[Scorch stands and shoves him\]

"Ah ha you Joker now?"

\[Shade shoves him back. Scorch steps back.\]

"Naw, but for real, you don't be listenin’. Always got yo head somewhere else. It's like ain't nuthin' get through."

"Eat ploor."

"Ah OOH HAHA I remember dat one from the old Batman."

"I thought you would."

\[Shade reaches under him, unzips his ruck, and pulls out two items.\]

\[There’s trebled fizz, followed by the signal popping of radio communication.\]

\[Scorch finds his pack and sits, still looking past, almost through Shade.\]

"And you talk about me not payin’ attention."

"Wha-whatt’d I do?"

"Yo ass looking at them trees again. What’s there?"

"Naw foo it's nuthin’... (that shit just like last night)."

"Huh? You mumble somethin over there?"

"Not a damn thing. You eatin’?"

\[Shade tossed a paperbag-toned ration at Scorch. He almost lets it drop, but catches it.\]

"You are too. C’mon."

"Let's see what we got here… pasta, oh okay day ain't bad. What you got?"

"Sloppy Joe."

"Uhhuu you gonna get dem's slop shit+farts again. Hahaha."

"Man nigga shut up." \[Shade goes to slap his head. Scorch dodges.\]

"My evasion too fast."

"It was barely fast enough during that Dare. Why'd you go for it? You burned one, so I had to burn, then Sun had to step in."

"Foo\! I already been told you Black ass twice, that one in the bush had a lock on us. Way too hard to bait out anyway. And then there was… there was—"

"There was what?"

\[They look at each other for a while. Rare to see them quiet. Their shadows slant and enlarge behind them. The camera pics up a low voice humming mid-song.\]

"Aw nvm, you wouldn't understand."

"Okay."

\[Quieter now. Amid the island ambience, I hear them chewing like cows, and that humming coming from off-screen. I can't place the song.\]

"It's so weird," Shade says.

"You gotta be more specific. Lots of weird shit going on here."

"Well there's the obvious shit: the war, Okinawa, soldiers of various ethnicities coming at us. They're not all Chinese. You saw?"

\[Scorch gulps down some water.\]

"Yeah I noticed."

"Yeah. But I meant it's weird how Sun just left this tablet while he went to do a perimeter check. I mean how long does it take to go around a 30 foot perimeter."

\[Scorch shakes his head.\]

"Dat nigga weird. Add him to the list."

"Imma add yo ass to this list." 

\[Shade reaches to slap him. Scorch slaps his head first. Shade dodges.\]

"Your speed stat got slow on."

"Whatever."

\[Shade picks up the radio.\]

"Yo Sun, what's good?"

"Fine. Was just listening to an old song."

"While on perimeter check?"

"I secured the perimeter twenty minutes ago."

"Oh."

"Y'all eatin?"

"Yeah, yeah. Got one for you."

"Good. Thought I smelled it. My mouth’s watering."

"I'll heat it up for you."

"Cool. I'm going back."

\[The radio clicks off. The humming continues, louder now. \*Thrummm-hummm-thrummm.\* Shade fumbles with Sun's ration packet, hands visibly trembling. Drops it twice before getting it open.\]

"You good?" Scorch asks, but he's not looking at Shade. Still watching the trees.

"Yeah. Dare burnout. You feel it?"

"Nah. I'm straight."

\[Scorch's hands are steady as he shovels pasta into his mouth. No visible tremor despite his Exposure readings having maxed during the triple Dare.\]

"It's so weird," Shade says again.

\[Scorch doesn't respond. Just chews. The firelight makes his face strange—half-shadow, half-orange glow. His eyes don't track with the conversation.\]

\[0031:45 \- Sun returns. Sits across from them. Sets the tablet between his knees, screen glowing blue-white. His fingers move across it: \*tap-tap-tap, swipe, tap.\* He doesn't make eye contact with either brother.\]

"Sun, bro, you gonna eat?"

"Mhm."

\[Sun doesn't reach for the heated ration. Keeps working the tablet. The humming gets quieter. Shade's breathing audible now—faster, shallower. \*Thump-thump-thump\* picks up on the camera's audio.\]

"Yo, this occupation shit is insane," Scorch says suddenly. His usual cadence starts strong but dies halfway through. "Like, okay the Chinese here, fine. But you feel it? That other thing?"

\[Shade leans forward slightly. Camera angle shifts with the movement.\]

"What other thing?"

"The presence. Like something's using the Chinese as cover, but it's watching from deeper. It's in the trees, the water, the air. It's in—" 

\[Scorch touches his chest, fingers pressing against his sternum.\] 

"—it's closer than it should be."

\[The fire \*crackles-pops\*, sends sparks into the dark. \*Ssssssss-crack.\* Shade's posture tightens—shoulders up, head forward.\]

"You sound paranoid."

"I sound right."

\[Sun's fingers stop. He looks up for the first time since sitting down. His gaze is direct, unblinking.\]

"He's not wrong," Sun says. "This island is occupied in more than one sense. Layers of control. The Chinese are the outer layer."

"And what's the inner layer?"

\[Sun tilts his head quizzically. A long pause— approximately 4 seconds— before responding. His expression is neutral, almost serene.\]

"Do you remember when we were kids? Building that Lego town in the old Lakeside house?"

\[Shade's head jerks slightly. Visible confusion in his posture. Scorch stops chewing mid-bite.\]

"Yeah," Shade says carefully. "We spent like, what, two months on it?"

"Two months and six days." \[Sun's voice is soft but measured.\] "We built it block by block. Every building, every street, every little park bench. We thought we were gods of that world."

\[Scorch laughs—short, sounds more like a cough than genuine amusement.\] 

"Man, I remember. I kept trying to add the Batcave and y'all kept saying it didn't fit the aesthetic."

"You still put it in the sewer system," Shade adds. \[His shoulders drop slightly. A flicker of warmth crosses his face—a half-smile that reaches his eyes for the first time since the recording began.\] "We found it like a week later."

\[For approximately 6 seconds, all three are smiling. Scorch nods, Shade's posture relaxes, Sun's expression softens. The fire warms. The jungle ambience quiets.\]

"Then Dad came down," Sun says.

\[Both smiles disappear instantly. Scorch's jaw clenches. Shade's back goes rigid.\]

"He didn't mean to step on it," Shade says quickly. His hands grip his knees. "He was looking for tools, he—"

"He crushed the town hall," Sun interrupts. \[His face shows no emotion.\] "The library. Half the residential district. We spent weeks on that, and in two seconds, it was rubble."

\[Scorch sets his ration down with more force than necessary. \*Thunk.\* Stares at the ground between his feet.\]

"The point," Sun continues, eyes back on the tablet, "\*is that we were builders. Small builders. And something larger walked through our world without even noticing. That's what's happening here. The Chinese, this presence Scorch feels—they're walking through \*our\* world. Crushing what we build."

\[Shade's hands form fists on his knees. His breathing changes—shorter, harder through his nose. Jaw muscles flex.\]

"So what," Shade says. \[Voice tight, clipped.\] "We just let them?"

\[Sun looks up. The firelight reflects in his eyes—bright, unnatural. He holds Shade's gaze for an extended moment without blinking.\]

"No. We become something they \*can't\* walk through. A force of comparable size. Comparable capability."

"You don't mean armies," Shade says. \[His voice is quieter now. Wary.\]

"No. I don't."

\[The humming returns. Louder. \*Thrummmmm-hummmmm.\* The camera's audio distorts slightly—a digital warble overlaying the sound.\]

"What you mean then?" \[Scorch's voice is higher pitched than usual. Almost childlike. He's staring at Sun with his head tilted, like trying to recognize someone from a distance.\]

\[Sun doesn't answer. Returns to the tablet. \*Tap-tap-swipe.\* His face is illuminated by the blue glow, features cast in sharp relief.\]

\[Shade opens his mouth. Closes it. Opens it again.\] 

"Dad would've—"

\[Scorch's entire body goes still. His face hardens—eyes flat, mouth a thin line. Sun doesn't look up. Doesn't stop working the tablet.\]

"—Dad would've told us to stick together. No matter what."

\[Silence. The fire \*hisses-spits.\* Wind moves through the clearing—or something else. \*Whoooosh-tap-tap-tap.\* Tree branches visible at the edge of frame sway though no breeze touches the flames.\]

"Dad's not here," Sun says finally. \[No inflection. Statement of fact.\] "And maybe that's the point."

\[Shade's shoulders slump. He looks down at his hands—still shaking—then at Scorch, then at Sun. His mouth opens as if to speak but nothing comes out. He swallows hard. Visible.\]

\[They eat in silence. The chewing sounds are amplified in the quiet: \*smack-chomp-smack, gulp, smack-chomp-smack.\* The humming weaves between their breaths—three distinct rhythms that don't quite sync.\]

\[Archivist's Note: At timestamp 0042:17, there is an audio anomaly. The humming stops abruptly. In the silence, three distinct breathing patterns are audible: Shade's (rapid, shallow), Scorch's (steady but occasionally hitching), and a third—thin, mechanical, emanating from the tablet's speaker. Sun had not spoken for approximately 90 seconds when this was recorded.\]

\[0042:19 \- Scorch suddenly stands. Points at the treeline with his whole arm extended, finger shaking.\]

"Yo. You hear that?"

"Hear what?" \[Shade's head whips toward where Scorch is pointing. Squints into the darkness.\]

"That voice. It's—" \[Scorch stops. Blinks rapidly three times.\] "Nah. Nvm."

"What voice?"

\[Scorch looks at Shade. Then his eyes drift past, unfocused, like looking through him at something behind.\]

"Your voice. Coming from the trees. You said... you said 'Foo you just said that. What the fuck.'"

\[Shade's entire body goes rigid. His eyes widen. Those were his exact words from approximately 23 minutes earlier at the post-battle treeline.\]

"I didn't say anything just now."

"I know. But I heard you."

\[The fire pops—\*CRACK-snap-hisssss.\* Sun's tablet screen flickers. For 2-3 frames, the reflection on Sun's face changes—the blue glow shifts to orange-red, highlights his features in a way that makes the shadows look burned, textured. Then normal blue light returns.\]

\[Sun stands, tucks the tablet under his arm. His movements are deliberate, controlled.\]

"We should sleep in shifts. I'll take first watch."

"Sun—" \[Shade half-rises, one hand extended.\]

"Rest. We move at 0600."

\[Sun walks to the edge of the clearing. Stands with his back to them, silhouetted against the darker jungle. The humming resumes—and the rhythm matches the rise and fall of Sun's shoulders as he breathes.\]

\[Scorch lies down quickly, pulls his knees to his chest. His eyes stay open, fixed on the fire. Doesn't blink for over a minute.\]

\[Shade remains seated between where his brothers are positioned. He looks left at Scorch. Right at Sun's back. Down at his still-shaking hands. His chest rises and falls—deep breath, hold, slow exhale. Trying to steady himself. It doesn't work.\]

\[0048:33 \- The last clear image before the timestamp cuts: the fire casting three shadows against the bunker wall. Two shadows are stationary—one curled (Scorch), one sitting upright (Shade). The third shadow—tall, standing at the clearing's edge—elongates and contracts in a rhythm that doesn't match the fire's movement. Sun's physical body remains perfectly still, but his shadow shifts. Grows. Shrinks. Grows again.\]

\[0048:37 \- Feed corruption. Audio continues for 4 more seconds: the humming, three sets of breathing (two human, one mechanical), and a sound like \*click-whirr-click\*—possibly the tablet, possibly something else.\]

\[0048:41 \- Recording ends.\]

\---

\*\*\[Archivist's Note \- Addendum\]:\*\*

This was the last footage recovered from Shade's helmet-mounted camera before the unit proceeded to Node 1 (Zamami Island). The three brothers were never recorded together in a single frame again. 

Subsequent mission logs reference "communication difficulties" and "unit cohesion concerns" but provide no detail. When questioned during debriefing (prior to his disappearance), Shade described this evening as "the last time we were in the same room but not in the same world."

The audio anomaly at 0042:17 has been analyzed by three separate technical teams. None can identify the source of the third breathing pattern. The tablet recovered from Sun's effects (see Evidence Log CLEF-7) contained no audio recording features and should not have been capable of producing the sound captured on this recording.

The shadow distortion visible at timestamp 0048:33 is likely a compression artifact. Likely.

## Shade \- Disappearance

The morning sun greeted my eyes, but only the sound of nature made itself known. Tree fronds, high and swishing, wind breezing to a blow, the sawing buzz of insects all pressed around me. But voices were missing. So were the disparate pops of discharging small arms. 

That wasn't the most disturbing part. Worse was that they'd been right there, at angles around me some time before dawn. But now all that remained where their sleep mats had been were moist dirt divots, outlines of their forms.

I searched around. Through the spines of jungle, my sweat-blistered feet chafed, cuts dragged across my forearms, but there was nothing.

Panic began to tear at my nerves at the possibilities: taken? captured? or killed?

I slapped my face, and forced it to quit the near crying contours. No bodies, no blood. "They're alive. They're alive." In this hell, I forced myself to believe it.

The morning now made itself known with its burning blades stabbing through the vegetation, and each lance of light thrust despair forward in my mind. I felt as if in the jaws of a great monster, prepared to be good for the day. Paranoid thoughts surveilled me as I circled the camp, and on the fourth spiral around I saw them. Two sets of uncovered footprints, headed in opposing directions.

## Scorch

This has got to be it. The right way. But the sun’s fuckin’ with my head so I got turned around a few times before gettin’ outta that godawful jungle.

There’s streets, shops, even people here (like normal). The battle is waay behind me, I think… What the fuck was that? Looked like that old man flashed a knife, somethin’ there in the waistband, ready to pull up. But no no, it’s just the sun’s gleam. That’s all, nothin’ to freak about.

The way these niggas look at me… goddamn it, it’s disturbing. Might as well be an alien here, from another planet. Can’t believe I signed up for this. The bag wasn’t bad, true. Being with the brothers was a decent bonus, but like always, they’re gone again. Could never rely on em’ somethings never change.

But fuck it, I’m good. I got me. Got the gun and deez cocoa-colored nuts. That’s all I need.

Wait. All uh-sudden I gotta shit. BAD. No worse… throw up.

I pull over to some fly-infested grass patch and let it out. It’s all green and brown mush from the pit of my gut and smells like the bathroom at the club at 3AM, all backed-up johns, smoky and shitty.

When I look up, that same ol’ man is lurkin’ again. Here he come. But his face is friendlier. He learned. Bout time. Now he callin’ me over, looks like he wants to help. Got hands behind his back but I ain’t scared. Got the gun, remember?

Next thing I know he’s leadin’ me toward a boat. Probably wanna show me his fish or something. I ask him if he seen two other Black folk, soldiers like me, he nods and beckons at the boat again. He’s dat’ nigga in the game waitin’ on you to press ‘A’ to get on.

But hold up—his lips moved when I asked. Did he answer? Couldn’t hear no words. Just a buzzing. Like TV static. Or maybe that’s my ears.

I feel more eyes on me, but ain’t nuthin’ new. Reflections stacked in glass windows. Scooters idling longer than they should. A woman with an umbrella staring too long. Maybe they all know. Maybe they all been watchin’.

I touch the metal behind my shirt—still there like always. They said he’d be on an island, the target. But mufucka didn’t tell which one. Why the military be ass like that sometimes?

Now my ass got bubbleguts. That combined with swampass in this heat is enough to sit man down for good minute. But not me. I finally press ‘A’.

The boat’s small but stable. Missing brothers, enemy-at-large, this horrible heat, war in my belching stomach and all around, that’s four problems too many for today.

And here’s the thing: I can talk. Always could. Talk my way into a party, talk my way outta cuffs, talk my way to a free drink when the night was dry. My mouth’s the real weapon, not this rusty piece. I could talk my way into God’s own good graces if I had to.

So I test it. Try to talk the old man. Ask him what he’s hiding behind his back. Ask him where he’s taking me. Ask him—ask him something.

He smiles, keeps walking. No answer. Just that hum again, louder this time. Flies buzzing, or maybe his throat working like a radio on the wrong channel.

I swallow hard. My voice don’t stick like it used to.

But nah. I’m good. I’m always good.

## Burned on the Beach (Bonta)

I’d been told to stay away from the beaches. These days, even the skies aren’t safe, that’s what 母親 said. Merciful heavens, even the skies\!

But today was unavoidable. The madness of the fire that scorched me still has me crying. But Meio was crying harder, so I suppose there’s nothing more I can complain for.

Mom had gone to do her business and told me to watch my little sister. This meant entertaining her as if she were a barking, sniffing 子犬, except there was no mess to clean up after her. 

"Big brother\! What are you doing?" Meio called. "Come over and help dig this up."

"In a minute." I was fixated by the horizon. I extended my arm to a point to count boats. "One… two…" I got to ten before I lost count. On the Awase side the floating masses were all fishing rigs, some small, others more robust. But here, they were war machines. ヤバイ\! Rumors on the radio were true\! This is what they called a "blockade" I thought. But I couldn’t put my finger on why.

Under a bright sky, choppy seas stuttered before me at the water’s expanse. Some wind tousled my hair and a jet’s cry overhead stuffed out my hearing for a spell. 

The slip of a second was the beginning of a new terror.

"Meio?"

I turned slowly. Yet the only thing that greeted me was a plastic pink pale and kiwi green shovel sworded into the sand. Her small footprints led back to the pirate ship.

"Meio? Where’d you go? You shouldn’t go so far."

My heart began pumping from the adventure afoot. I felt as if I were Prendick first glimpsing Moreau’s isle of Beast-freaks. I leaned in and began to track her.

The pirate ship was always one of my favorite features of this beach. I imagined it running the rough waters, tossed here and there along the unforgiving waves of the Pacific, her pursued crew, desperate, then marooned here, long before Chatan-町 had grown to encircle it. What fantasy\! I thought of this as I weaved through the plastic hold, climbed the ladder to the crow’s nest and surveyed the area. That’s when the sharp realization made my heart thunder with a new piercing gallop.

Meio is missing. My heart clattered, my skin prickled, my voice shriveled into sand.

"Meio\! This isn’t funny come out\!"

At that point, movement on the beach caught my eye. One of the boats, a smaller but still hardy vessel, had moved near the shore during my search. 怖い\! I immediately ducked. My breath came in quick wheezes. Something told me not to be found. But where was Meio? The situation went south fast. As silent as possible, I lowered myself to the main deck and concealed myself again.

Convinced I couldn’t be seen, I peak out over the edge of the ship. Across the between, where no more than ten minutes prior I’d been standing, a group of men had gathered.   
I pointed my finger and made tiny pokes at air, counting. Perhaps there were a dozen. I heard one, a Black guy with a craggly white and black beard shout and gesture around, then the others fanned out along the area. They were passing right by the ship\!

My heart jammed my throat. Stomach pressed flat, chest thumped, sweat ticked down my ear. Gadzooks — even my own breath threatened to expose me to these soldiers of unknown allegiance or intention.

For some time I waited, until the sandy footsteps fell away. When I got up there were only three men left by the boat. One was covered in a thick, tarp-like hood, the other was the 黒人 who gave the orders, and another appeared Chinese, but gripped the wrist of a little girl.

Meio\!

"Found her over by the basketball court bathrooms," he said. He thrust her forward toward the hooded man.

"This is no place for children. Not anymore," the black guy said, "what should we do with her C-1? She might tattle."

"No, no I-I won’t tell. I’ll be good," Meio’s slight frame rattled.

The hooded man stepped forward. "There are few child subjects on the Zamami node. She may do well there."

"Two birds with one stone?" The bearded soldier fingered his weapon.

"Precisely."

"Well?"

"Get away from her\!" I rushed up as the hooded man reached out to her. In a flash of sungleam, two guns were aimed at me. I anticipated being cut down at any moment. But I heard no pops or bangs. As I ducked my shoulder to tackle him, I noticed how the air moved around him, all distorted and blurry. Then there was the heat. Sweaty, humid, boiling. Like standing over a stove, windows shut, no air, no mercy. Heat stacked on heat, execrable heat, profane heat— too much for flesh to bear.

That’s when his hand reached out and grabbed my wrist with magma intensity.

"Wh-what AAH\!" The bestial バケもの burned me\!

Through my wet vision, I saw the igneous burning of the veiny lava flows of his wrist. The texture was that of the cutting coral shoals surrounding tide pools and housing all manner of sea-life surrounding Okinawa. Pain kept me from outright pandemonium—skosh short of madness. But after he swung me to the surface I rolled away, clutching my smoking forearm in agony. Burned, broken, dizzy, I looked up.

"Meio\! No\!"

The boat cleaved the waves, motor ripping the air, my sister was their prisoner.

I failed.

## Sun’s Reasons

"When you’re out of your element there is forbidden energy all around. Part of you waits to be regulated as refuse, and no amount of soothing can bring order to swinging madness."

"You say this as if it’s an absolute."

"Because I know it’s true."

"How so?"

"Take him, Cinder as an example: once he revolted in his country of fate, he has been reborn into sanguine form. Did you see the way he flung that kid into coarse sand?"

"Quite brutal, it was."

"I don’t disagree, however, this is the way of the world. Those who dare to go beyond their appointed share reap larger gains. These gains come in the form of choice: freedom enduring."

"I suppose you’re not wrong. But what form of freedom do you want?"

"Hmm. Do you know about banshees?"

"Sure. Loud, shrieking, sometimes ghastly, occasionally describes a woman displaying obnoxious behavior. That sort of creature?"

"Haha, yes yes. Were you aware they were harbingers of death in Irish folklore?"

\---

"I wasn’t."

"I hear her or an equivalent spirit of these islands—"

"Like the 船幽霊 (funayūrei), the ship ghosts, believed to be spirits of the drowned here?"

"Uh, yes like them. The cries of the banshee foretell of a coming mob of ignorance, like a rising wave that threatens to inundate us all. But people like him have isolated themselves from the coming calamity, his power is awesomely terrifying."

"Terrifying? Do I sense fear?"

"I do hope so. I said there wasn’t, a whiff of it. But once we get there and go through with the procedure, there’s no going back."

"What about the brothers? Do you think they’ll get it?"

"…perhaps maybe no… I don’t know, they may never understand it. (Sigh) I just hope they know that I’m no creep or freak. That I did all this to make us better, stronger. The system will only accept cosmic inputs. As I am, I can’t provide that at the moment. Yet once I get so endured, then there’s no stopping turning the universe of humanity and beyond."

## Chinese Massage

Lying on the table smashed under a panting Chinese dude’s press is not how I normally prefer to brunch on a Saturday. The whole experience is less than ideal. 

The looping music. Low lights. Super-perfumed air. Room the size of a burial tomb. Yep, the whole arrangement sums to a lousy— scratch that— "unfavorable" scenario that I *get* to experience. I tell myself that as the masseuse (whose credentials are dubious) drags his knuckles down the muscle of my back with one hand while the other? Well it’s conveniently stabilizing him as it skirts the flesh of my mashed breast. Is that routine? I wince as he completes the pass and changes sides.

The ability to endure this hell buys me many things. Most important of which is a few more days of unimpeded movement. Take the pain to have a little less pain later. Without the teeth-grinding. It’s like that.

On a locked down island like this, that’s essential. Then I also get closer to a shot at them, and saving my life in the process. A girl’s gotta have goals. Just so happens mine pair best with meaty hands swirling my buttocks and the power-pills in my fake purse. Fake leather. Real salvation.

My head’s between his crotch now as he rings the back of my neck with cat-strangling force. Then there’s a digital bleating from the side. My phone.

I shoot my head up and hear a surprisingly mousey wail come from the well-built Chinese dude in the black shirt. He hisses and curses in Mandarin, while cupping his junk.

I answer the phone.

"Yeah?"

"They’re on the move. Headed to Zamami. Are you covered?"

I snatch a look down at my topless form under the tiny towel.

"You could say that."

"This is no time for Koi-ness. Yes or no?"

A pause. The masseuse is breathing too close behind me. The smell of cigarette pricks through the garden scent air. 

"Yes, yes of course I am."

"Good. Transportation’s arranged. Kadena Marina. 1800\. They’ll take you."

"Got it."

"Watch yourself. The first freak is with him."

"I thought I smelled something burning."

"Heh you were right. Once you arrive, I’ll be in touch. No mistakes this time."

"Same to you."

"Cute."

I end the call. The masseuse is just standing there, patient. Then I drop the towel, sit up and reach for my bra and dress.

"No, no no, no\!" He covers his eyes and sidles out of the room, red-faced.

Well there goes his tip.

## [Archivist's Note \#2](https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/11DCxvn3lqAYl5mvK3-EPIeRkbn_lhdcHbBJhBh6H31M/edit)

Account \#471 \- Rukiko Yonabaru, Age 46 \- Kitanakagusuku City

\*Emotion: Grief\*

Why do we have to hide again? They told us PLA soldiers were patrolling the streets. That's the reason they gave, though they didn't have to. 

I saw the trucks, heavy with guns, colored a muck-green. The smell of fuels made me gag before we fled to the high caves. Just as in all the old war stories, we became prisoners on our own island as the elders had. But I had no family to protect. Are, the angelic dog, ran at the rumble of the massive tires. I haven't seen him in hours.

My heart is sad at my failure.

\---

The page felt heavier than it should. Not metaphorically— actually heavy, as if the words themselves had mass and the testimony of Rukiko Yonabaru carried the weight of her missing dog, her failed protection, her inherited fear. I set it down carefully, a surgeon placing a diseased organ on the tray. My fingers traced the edges— once, twice, three times— aligning them with the stack beneath until the corners kissed perfectly.

Are. Such a small name for such a large absence.

The "Emotion" label caught my eye like a fish hook. Blue ballpoint, recent. Not my father's hand— his was cramped and tiny, four letters crushed together like refugees in a boat. This was someone else's taxonomy of grief, someone who thought sorrow could be filed and categorized and \*labeled\* like a specimen in that same insectary where I'd first begun my descent. 

Who had done this? The manifest said nothing about emotional coding. The IGET documentation was silent. Yet here it sat, clinical and cold: \*Emotion: Grief.\* As if that explained anything. As if that contained the dog named Are and the caves and the smell of fuel that made a woman gag.

I picked up my pen. Tapped it against the margin— \*tap tap tap\*— a rhythm that answered the boxes' earlier chorus. Started to write. Crossed it out. The ink bled slightly on the crossing-out, a small black wound. Started again.

\*Check: Who added emotional categories? Not in manifest. Purpose? Control? Or something else?\*

The next account waited like a patient with a terminal diagnosis.

\---

\*\*Account \#302 \- Tatsuya Hiroo, Age 40, Naha City\*\*

\*Emotion: Satisfaction\*

We heard them in the old Shuri Castle tunnels. The ones they said were sealed after the reconstruction. But they were never sealed, not really. My son found the entrance behind the foundation stones. He went in with his friends. 

Only he came back out.

He wouldn't say what he saw. Just kept saying it over and over: "They're satisfied now. They're satisfied now."

He stopped eating three days later.

\---

Satisfaction.

The word was a stone in my mouth. Heavy and wrong-shaped, metallic-tasting. I bit my lip— old habit, grounding technique— and tasted copper. A child stops eating and the archivist (not me, someone before me, someone who handled these papers with different hands) writes \*Satisfaction\* like it's a reasonable conclusion. Like satisfaction and starvation share the same root system.

My margin note grew thorns as I rewrote it:

\*Labeling system is WRONG. Deliberately wrong? Check all emotional categories against content. Is this misdirection? Is this their language or ours?\*

The boxes behind me settled with a long, drawn-out groan— the sound of old joints, old wood, old secrets trying to get comfortable in their confinement. I didn't turn around. Didn't need to. I could feel their attention, that portrait-with-traveling-eyes sensation that had been my companion since the delivery men left. 

The shortest account sat on top of the pile like a gravestone.

\---

\*\*Account \#089 \- Anonymous \- Kin Town\*\*

\*Emotion: Remorse\*

The darkness remembers.

\---

That was all. Five syllables. The page had nothing else— no explanation, no context, just that phrase sitting there like a curse or a prayer or both. I turned it over. Nothing. Flipped it back. The darkness remembers. The paper was thicker than the others, textured like skin. My fingers didn't want to hold it.

Because I'd seen this before.

Not this account— I would have remembered this account, this brevity, this horror— but the phrase itself. It lived somewhere in the stack I'd already cataloged, nested in another testimony like a parasite. I rifled through the papers, my careful organization collapsing as documents scattered across the desk in a way that made my chest tighten. There. Account \#213. A woman from Chatan— her daughter's nightmares— \*She keeps saying 'the darkness remembers, mama.' She's never heard the old stories. How does she know that phrase?\*

And here in \#089, stated as fact. No question mark. No fear. Just: \*The darkness remembers.\*

Two separate witnesses. Same exact words. The daughter shouldn't know. The anonymous source from Kin Town offers no explanation of \*what\* remembers or \*how\* or \*why\*.

I stood. Too fast. The chair scraped— a sound like fingernails on slate. The papers needed reorganizing. The sequence was broken. Type B civilian accounts were supposed to flow chronologically but someone had misfiled \#089 or I had or the boxes themselves had shuffled their contents while I slept. While I wasn't looking. While I \*blinked\*.

I needed to think. To connect. I'd read so much in three days— Bonta's diary about the arrested silence, that mean quiet that preceded the invasion. The boy who'd described the clouds forming an inverted pyramid, pointing west. Ships appearing like coffins without crew. Half-men, half-shadows walking ashore. Moreau's vivisected creatures made real.

And then the military files. The brothers.

Shade in the middle— the practical one, the one whose helmet cam had captured everything. Scorch the youngest— already obsessed with something in the trees during that first beach battle. The way he'd looked at the jungle like it was looking back. And Sun, the oldest, always distant, always working that tablet, talking in riddles about becoming something that couldn't be crushed.

The Dare system. That's what they'd called it. Some kind of augmentation protocol where they burned Energy and pushed their bodies past human limits until their Exposure meters screamed red. I'd read the technical specs but they felt thin, inadequate. The footage told a different story— the Triple Dare they'd executed on that beach, the \*Daybreak Trinity\*, where all three brothers synchronized and the air itself warped. The way time stuttered. The way Scorch's weapon had jammed and Shade had saved him and Sun had orchestrated it all with that calm, detached voice.

But it was after the battle that things went wrong.

The campfire footage. I'd watched it twice, read the transcript four times. The way Scorch kept staring at the trees. The way he'd heard Shade's voice coming from the jungle— \*"Foo you just said that. What the fuck"\*— the exact words Shade had spoken twenty-three minutes earlier. An echo. Or a recording. Or something worse.

And Sun. Sun talking about the Lego town their father had destroyed. \*"We become something they can't walk through. A force of comparable size. Comparable capability."\* Not armies. Something else. The humming that had started then, three breathing patterns woven together— two human, one mechanical. The shadow that moved wrong while Sun's body stayed perfectly still.

That was the last time the three brothers appeared together in a single frame. After that: separation. Fragmentation. The mission logs referenced "communication difficulties" and "unit cohesion concerns" but provided no detail. Shade had described it during his debriefing (before he disappeared) as "the last time we were in the same room but not in the same world."

And there was a woman's name that kept appearing. Timeera Koi. Mother? Operative? The files contradicted themselves. She was alive. She was dead. She was everywhere and nowhere. An anchor that had failed or been cut loose.  
And then there was Timeera Koi.  
Eight accounts mentioned her. Eight completely incompatible versions. Mother. Operative. Victim. Witness. Anchor. Alive on Kadena on March 3rd. Dead on Ishigaki on March 1st— two days after her confirmed death. The timeline was a shattered mirror, and she was the largest shard.  
One document stood out: an intercepted communication log from a massage parlor in Naha. Civilian cover. The transcript was brief, clinical— someone (Koi?) arranging transport to Zamami just as Shade's mission logs indicated he was headed there. The voice on the other end had warned about "the first freak." Cinder, maybe? The files referenced a C-Unit operative known for thermal signatures.  
But whose side was she on? The phone call suggested she was tracking the brothers, but the word "them" could mean anything. Protecting? Hunting? Containing?  
My father had underlined her name in three separate reports, always with a question mark. Never an explanation. As if he'd died still trying to figure out what she was.  
I smoothed the pages flat. Once. Twice. My hands were shaking.

I needed the rest of Type A. The military files that would explain what happened after the brothers separated. Where Shade went (Zamami Island, Node 1). Where Scorch ended up (Tokashiki, fever and rage according to the fragments). Where Sun...

Where Sun had become whatever he'd become.

The wall of boxes sagged against the far side of my study like exhausted soldiers. Most I'd already opened— their contents metastasizing across every surface, my once-clean space now infected with paper and photographs and maps marked with routes that led nowhere. Five boxes remained. I'd been systematic. Clinical. Type B first (civilian), then Type A (military), then cross-reference until the truth emerged like a fossil from stone.

But the truth wasn't emerging. It was \*multiplying\*.

I approached the remaining boxes. That compressed heat rolled off them— not imagination, not stress-induced hallucination, but actual physical warmth like standing too close to a furnace. The dank Okinawa smell had intensified over the past three days, that corrosive perfume seeping out through cardboard pores. My study didn't smell like my study anymore. It smelled like caves. Like salt water and copper and that other thing— that \*meat\* smell that had no business coming from paper.

I reached for the second box from the left.

My fingers found the lid seam and pulled.

Nothing.

Not locked. Not taped. But \*fused\*. The cardboard had melted together or calcified or become one continuous surface, the seam still visible but non-functional, like a scar that had healed too tightly. I pulled harder. Wedged my fingers into the gap. The space was there— I could see it, a shadow-line where the lid should separate— but my hands wouldn't fit. Or the box wouldn't let them.

The box resisted me like a living thing protecting its organs.

Water damage, I told myself. The manifest mentioned flooding. 2034\. The Kadena facility. Moisture would do this— warp the cardboard, seal it shut through some chemical process I didn't understand but could research, could \*solve\*, because that's what I did. I solved puzzles. I made order from chaos. I was trained for this.

But the box was dry. Desert-dry. And warm. Always warm.

I stepped back. Retrieved my notepad. Wrote carefully, each letter a small act of control:

\*Box 4A (second from left) \- inaccessible. Possible water damage (though box is dry?). Requires specialist tools or conservator. Contact Tanaka or Lt. Chen. Not urgent.\*

The last two words were a lie so obvious even the pen seemed to protest, the ink flowing thicker, darker. Because this \*was\* urgent. The sealed box sat there like a missing tooth, a gap in the smile of information, and every instinct I had— archival training, curiosity, grief, rage— screamed that what I needed was \*in there\*. 

Sun's final logs, maybe. The explanation for why the brothers' voices had started echoing back from the trees. The truth about that humming, those three breathing patterns, that mechanical third presence on the tablet. The records that would tell me if Scorch had survived the fever, if Shade had made it past Node 1, if Sun had completed whatever transformation he'd started around that campfire.

Or something worse than records. Something that explained why the box sealed itself. Why it stayed silent while the others hummed and breathed and \*watched\*.

I set my pen down. Perpendicular to the notepad. Parallel to the desk edge. Order. Control. These were my weapons against the chaos.

That's when the boxes began to speak.

\---

\*Tap.\*

Small. Dismissible. The kind of sound cardboard makes when temperature shifts or humidity changes or a building settles into its foundation. I'd heard it before— constant low percussion since the delivery. Background noise. Ignorable.

I turned back to my desk. Back to Bonta's diary where he'd described the silence before the invasion. That arrested silence. Mean and waiting.

\*Tap. Tap.\*

Sharper now. Localized. Coming from the left side of the wall where the oldest boxes slouched. Not settling. Not random. The rhythm was too regular, too \*purposeful\*, like Morse code or a heartbeat or fingernails drumming against the inside of a coffin lid.

My pen hovered over Account \#089. The darkness remembers. The ink began to bleed slightly where I'd been holding the nib too long in one spot.

\*Tap tap tap.\*

I turned. Slowly. The boxes sat exactly as before— sagging, defeated, innocent. The sealed box second from the left, silent as a grave. But the tapping continued, and now I could pinpoint the source: the box on the far left, the one I'd opened first three days ago. The one that had contained the beach battle footage. The one that smelled like meat.

"Temperature change," I said aloud. My voice sounded thin and small, a child's voice in an empty house. "Humidity. Expansion and contraction. Completely normal."

The tapping stopped.

The silence was a held breath. Was a pause before the fall. Was Bonta's arrested silence made manifest in my study. Worse— infinitely worse— than the noise.

I counted to ten. Slowly. Clinical. The climate control hummed its steady mechanical lullaby. Outside, a car passed on the street below, normal and alive and \*outside\* where the world still made sense. I exhaled. Picked up my pen. Started to write a note about auditory hallucinations and stress and—

\*Tap-tap-tap-tap-tap.\*

From the right side of the wall this time. Faster. Insistent. A different box entirely, and before I could process that, another box joined in from the left. Then another. Then another. Until four boxes were tapping in sequence, in \*conversation\*, a rhythm that sounded almost like—

Like footsteps walking a perimeter.

Like something circling, looking for weakness, testing the boundaries of its cardboard prison. Like Sun doing his perimeter check while the humming started.

My pen fell. The clatter was sharp and immediate. The tapping stopped— all four boxes at once, synchronized, as if they'd heard me. As if they'd been \*listening\*.

Just like the helmet cam. Just like that moment when Scorch went still and said \*"Yo. You hear that?"\* and heard Shade's voice coming from the trees even though Shade was sitting right there.

The silence pressed down like deep water. Like standing at the bottom of the ocean. My ears rang with the absence of sound. Even the climate control seemed to have quit, or maybe I'd gone deaf, or maybe sound itself had been password-protected and I'd forgotten the code, just like Bonta's radios and scooters and vending trucks.

Then: \*Thummmmm.\*

Not a tap. A hum. Low and resonant, vibrating up through the floor and into my bones and teeth. Coming from all the boxes at once— except one.

The sealed box sat silent. Dead. Judgmental.

While the others hummed and thrummed like organs in a body, that one remained perfectly, terribly still. The control specimen. The unopened variable. The thing I couldn't access no matter how much I needed to.

The hum rose in pitch. Became almost musical. Almost like—

Three distinct patterns woven together: one fast and shallow (Shade's panic breathing), one steady but hitching (Scorch's pain), one thin and mechanical (the tablet that shouldn't breathe). The same patterns from the campfire footage. The same patterns from the night everything went wrong.

"Stop it," I whispered.

The humming intensified. The boxes swelled— I saw it, actually watched their cardboard sides bulge slightly like lungs inflating. That compressed heat rolled off them in waves that made the air shimmer. The smell hit next: copper and salt and \*meat\*, that rot-stink of flesh left too long in tropical heat, even though these were only papers, only documents, only \*words\*.

But boxes don't breathe. Boxes don't hum. Boxes don't \*remember\*.

Except these did.

I couldn't move. My body had locked down— prey animal stillness, the hindbrain taking over from the thinking mind. My pulse hammered in my throat with black-night highway speed worry, just like Bonta's pulse, just like my father's must have been in those final days, surrounded by these same boxes, listening to them whisper and hum and demand.

The sealed box stayed silent. Watching. Patient. Waiting for me to understand what Sun had meant when he said \*"We become something they can't walk through."\*

My feet carried me forward. I didn't decide to move. My body simply acted, pulled by a force deeper than reason or training or self-preservation. Three steps across my study— past my desk, past the scattered papers, past the point where I could pretend this was normal. My hand reached out toward the nearest humming box while the sealed box radiated that terrible patience in my peripheral vision, and I knew— \*knew\*— that this was the moment I'd been moving toward since the delivery men left, since I'd signed the release forms, since I'd first seen my father's four-letter signature at the foot of a witness statement.

This was the slow slide down into the antlion's trap. The greedy fine grains shifting beneath me.

I touched the cardboard.

It was hot. Feverish. \*Alive.\*

The humming stopped. All of them at once— instant silence. Even the climate control. Even my own breath caught and held. In that void of sound— that arrested silence, that mean quiet— I heard it:

A voice. Thin and distant. Coming from inside the box beneath my palm. Words I couldn't make out but inflection I recognized— the cadence of explanation, of justification, of a man trying to make his daughter understand why he'd done what he'd done. Why he'd chosen to witness. Why he'd let them separate.

My father's voice.

My fingers found the lid seam. Started to lift. And as the cardboard separated with a sound like tearing skin, I thought of Scorch staring at the trees and saying \*"Dis place is fucked. We gotta get off. Go home."\* I thought of Sun's shadow growing and shrinking while his body stayed still. I thought of Account \#089, that single line sitting in my mind like a warning I'd ignored:

\*The darkness remembers.\*

# Node 1

## [Ferry Drift](https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/15yu_tvU3puV1wm_pDqkNhzyxr0xz5LPMrOD3PfRNLwA/edit)

\*\*Field Report: K-97 "Mother/Knife"\*\*    
\*\*Mission Log: Zamami Transit\*\*    
\*\*Date: \[REDACTED\]\*\*

\---

Ferries weren't supposed to feel like coffins, but that one tried. The smell of diesel hung low and thick, clinging to the back of my throat like a greasy film. The seats shone with old sweat — vinyl worn to a slick patina by years of bodies that didn't want to be there. The few other passengers wore the same glazed look as livestock waiting to be herded off at market. Too quiet. Too still. Even the elderly woman three rows up hadn't moved in twenty minutes, hands folded in her lap like she was already practicing for the grave.

Normal enough, I told myself. That was always the danger cue.

The water beyond the salt-crusted windows looked wrong. Not rough — we were past typhoon season — but \*dark\*. The kind of dark that swallowed light instead of reflecting it. Okinawa's waters used to be that tourist-brochure turquoise, the shade that made mainlanders fork over their savings for a long weekend. Now it looked like old blood mixed with engine oil. Occupation did that. Turned paradise into a crime scene and made everyone complicit just by staying alive.

I catalogued exits out of habit: main door (forward, currently blocked by a sailor smoking despite the NO SMOKING sign), emergency hatch (starboard, rusted shut from the looks of it), windows (too small, but breakable in a pinch). Three potential hostiles visible — two sailors, one ticket-taker who moved like he'd done military time. The rest were civilians. Probably.

The loudspeaker crackled to life. Captain's voice, bored and nasal: "Departure in two minutes. All passengers remain seated—" 

The audio cut. Just for a second. A hitch in the broadcast like someone had pressed pause. When it resumed, the captain's cadence was slightly off. Flatter. Like he was reading phonetically from a script he didn't understand.

I noted the time: 1342 hours. First anomaly.

Two minutes past cast-off, the "normal" shattered.

Boots stormed the deck — heavy, arrhythmic, the clatter of men who'd learned to march but forgotten why. A half-dozen soldiers spilled aboard from a patrol boat that pulled alongside, late and loud, as if discipline had been left behind on the pier. They wore Japanese Self-Defense Force uniforms, but the patches were wrong. Stitched too recent, colors too bright. The kind of costume work that passed at a distance but fell apart under scrutiny.

They fanned out to posts like props in a half-rehearsed stage play. One positioned himself near the bow, rifle slung too low. Another blocked the bathroom corridor, picking at his fingernails with the edge of a combat knife. The rest scattered with no clear tactical purpose, more interested in looking imposing than actually securing anything.

One of them made me pause.

Too short. Five-foot-nothing, maybe less. Shoulders slouched forward like he was trying to compress himself into a smaller target. He kept tugging at his mask strap where it bit into his ear, adjusting and re-adjusting in a way that screamed \*discomfort\* instead of \*routine\*. His rifle hung the way a broom did in a child's hands — awkward, unfamiliar, like he'd been handed it five minutes ago and told to "look military."

His boots were two sizes too big. I could see the gap at the heel.

I made a note: \*Possible infiltrator. Age 14-16. Threat level: minimal. Comedy value: moderate.\*

\---

Diplomat papers got me waved through the first layer of inspection. A young soldier — actual PLA by the accent, mainland Mandarin with Beijing edges — scanned my documents with a handheld reader that beeped twice and flashed green. His eyes lingered on my face, then dropped. Chest. Hips. Back up when he remembered he was supposed to be professional.

I let him look. It bought me three extra seconds while his brain rebooted.

"Purpose of travel?" His voice came out slightly higher than intended.

"Agricultural consultation." I leaned forward just enough. The blouse wasn't cut low, but geometry did the rest. "Wild boar population assessment. Zamami's had complaints."

His gaze stuttered. Tried to stay on my face. Failed. "I... uh. Yes. Paperwork is—" He cleared his throat, handed the documents back too quickly, fingers fumbling. "—in order. You may proceed."

I smiled. Not warm. Just enough to let him know I'd noticed.

He flushed red to his hairline.

Behind him, an older soldier — the one with the scar bisecting his left eyebrow — watched the exchange with the dead-eyed patience of someone who'd seen it all before. He didn't look at me the way the young one did. He looked at me the way you'd look at a weapon someone left on the table. Functional. Dangerous. Requiring attention.

I filed his face away. That one would be a problem if things went sideways.

\---

I made a show of heading for the bathroom, weaving through the narrow aisle as another static burst cut through the loudspeaker. This time the captain's voice didn't return immediately. In the gap, I heard something else. Faint. Childlike. A snatch of melody that cut off before I could place it.

Second anomaly. 1349 hours.

The bathroom corridor reeked of industrial cleaner — the kind that stripped paint along with grime — and something sharper underneath. Sweat. Fear. And something else. Something organic and wrong.

Burned meat.

I paused. Inhaled carefully through my nose. There. Faint but unmistakable. The sickly-sweet char of seared flesh, recent enough that the smell hadn't fully dissipated into the ferry's general miasma of diesel and disinfectant.

One soldier had been in there too long. Suspicious. Or injured. Or both.

I knocked once. No answer.

I opened the door anyway.

\---

The kid jerked upright at the sink, hands braced against the porcelain like he'd been about to vomit. His eyes went wide behind the mask — actual terror, the kind that freezes limbs and stops breath. He was wearing a jacket. Thin windbreaker, completely wrong for the humid ferry heat. The sleeves hung past his wrists.

Covering something.

I shut the door behind me. Locked it.

"Relax," I said. "If I wanted you dead, you'd be bleeding already."

He didn't relax. His hand drifted toward the rifle slung across his back, then stopped halfway like he'd remembered he didn't know how to use it. His other arm stayed pressed against his torso, protective.

I reached out and peeled the mask off. He flinched but didn't resist — just a whimper caught in his throat, body going rigid.

Bonta Higa stared back at me. Fifteen, maybe sixteen. Round face, soft jaw still carrying baby fat, eyes too large and wet with barely-suppressed panic. The kind of kid who probably got good grades and helped old ladies cross the street before occupation turned childhood into a liability.

"Guess physical requirements aren't what they used to be," I said.

His mouth worked soundlessly. Then, in broken English that crashed between languages: "How—how you know I not real soldier?"

I grabbed his jacket collar, pulled him closer. He gasped — pain, sharp and immediate. His left arm jerked, and I caught the edge of something beneath the sleeve. Angry red, blistered. The smell intensified.

"Because real soldiers don't smell like barbecue," I said. Kept my voice flat. Clinical. "And they don't wince when someone touches them."

His eyes darted left, right, looking for escape routes that didn't exist. "I—it was accident. Cooking accident. Very clumsy, me."

"Show me."

"What? No, I—"

I didn't ask again. Just grabbed his left wrist and yanked the sleeve up.

The wound was fresh. Angry. Wrong.

Not a burn from fire or stove or any conventional heat source. The pattern was too specific — radiating lines that looked almost \*textured\*, like someone had pressed molten coral against his skin and held it there. The edges were cauterized, but the center still wept clear fluid. Thermal contact, high intensity, direct application. The kind of precision that only came from weaponized augmentation.

I'd seen burns like this before. In briefing photos. Classified reports with black bars and clinical language that tried to make horror sound routine.

Champion operative. Thermal class.

The kid was staring at his own arm like it belonged to someone else, breathing hard through his nose. "Please don't—don't tell them. I need to get to Zamami. I need—" His voice cracked. "Please."

I let go of his wrist. He yanked the sleeve down, clutching his arm against his chest.

"Who did this to you?"

"I told you, cooking—"

"Kid." I leaned in. Close enough that he had to look at me. "I've seen a lot of injuries. That's not from a stove. That's not from anything normal. So either you tell me what happened, or I drag you to those soldiers outside and let \*them\* ask."

His face went pale. Lips trembled. For a second I thought he might actually crack, spill everything in a panicked teenage confession.

Then his eyes dropped.

Not to my face. Lower.

I watched it happen in real-time: fear-brain shutting down, lizard-brain taking over. Fifteen-year-old hormones overriding survival instinct. His gaze locked on my chest, lingered, then drifted down like it had weights attached.

I let him stare for exactly two seconds. Timed it. Then:

"Your gaze has a gravity problem. Eyes up, kid."

His head snapped up so fast I heard his neck crack. Face went nuclear-red, sweat beading on his forehead. "I—I wasn't—I mean—sorry\! Sorry sorry sorry\!" He covered his eyes with both hands, wincing as the burned arm protested the movement. "Very sorry, onee-san\! Was not looking at—I mean, was not meaning to—ah, dame dame dame—"

"Save it." I straightened, put distance between us. Professional. Cold. "You've got bigger problems than being a teenage idiot. Someone burned you. Recently. And you're on a military ferry to Zamami in a stolen uniform. That's either incredibly brave or incredibly stupid."

He lowered his hands slowly, still not quite meeting my eyes. Safer to look at the wall. "Is... is both. Maybe."

"Why Zamami?"

His jaw clenched. "My grandmother. She sick. Very sick."

The lie sat there between us, brittle and obvious. But underneath it, something else bled through. Desperation. Real fear. The kind that made kids do suicidally stupid things.

Before I could press further, the loudspeaker rattled alive. The captain's voice, but wrong again — too fast, words bleeding together:

"Attention all passengers unscheduled security inspection remain in your seats do not move inspection teams will proceed compartment by compartment—"

No pause for breath. No natural rhythm. Like someone had spliced the announcement together from separate recordings.

Third anomaly. 1353 hours.

Boots thundered in the corridor. Close. Getting closer.

\---

"Shit." I grabbed Bonta by the collar — carefully, avoiding the burned arm — and shoved him into the stall. "Get in. Shut up. Make a sound and I'll tell them exactly where you are."

"But—"

"\*Now.\*"

He scrambled into the stall, jacket bunching awkwardly. I heard him fumble with the lock, curse under his breath in Japanese, fumble again.

I straightened my blouse, checked my reflection. Diplomat. Consultant. Bored professional on a routine trip. The mask slipped on easy as breathing.

The door slammed open.

Two soldiers. Real ones this time — older, harder-faced, with the dead eyes of men who'd stopped pretending this was peacekeeping. The lead one had a scar bisecting his left eyebrow, puckered and keloid-thick. Same one who'd watched me earlier.

His gaze swept the bathroom, clinical and thorough. Didn't linger on my body the way the younger soldier had. Just assessed threats, calculated odds, moved on.

That made him dangerous.

"Papers," he said. Mandarin with Okinawan vowels underneath. Local recruit.

I handed them over, movements slow and deliberate. Non-threatening. The second soldier — younger, twitchier, definitely staring now — kept his rifle pointed at my midsection. Safety off. Finger on the guard, not the trigger. Trained but green.

Scar scanned the documents. His eyes flicked to me. Back to the photo. Back to me. Taking his time.

I waited. Let him look. Professional interest only — he was checking for discrepancies, not appreciating the view. But the younger one behind him was doing both, gaze skating down then jerking back up when I met his eyes.

"Purpose of travel?"

"Agricultural consultation. Wild boar assessment." I kept my voice bored. Routine. "Your captain has the full manifest."

"Captain doesn't tell us shit." He flipped through the pages, squinting at the stamps. Then, switching to Mandarin: "\*Where did you study?\*"

Testing me.

"\*Beijing Foreign Studies University,\*" I lied smoothly. "\*Class of 2019\. Professor Wang's program.\*"

His expression didn't change, but something shifted behind his eyes. That was a real program. A real professor. The forgery included biographical depth most operatives didn't bother with.

He handed the papers back. "Wait outside."

\---

They searched the bathroom with the efficiency of men who'd done this a hundred times. Checked the stalls, the cistern, even ran their hands along the ceiling panels looking for contraband taped to the underside.

Bonta stayed silent. Credit where due — the kid had decent survival instincts when terror overrode hormones.

The younger soldier lingered at the locked stall door. Frowned. "Locked?"

My pulse didn't change. "Broken latch. Been like that since we boarded."

He rattled it once. Twice. The door held.

Scar grunted. "Move on. We've got twelve more compartments."

They filed out. The corridor boots faded.

I counted to thirty before unlocking the stall.

Bonta spilled out, face flushed, breathing hard through his mouth. "That was—that was—" He clutched his chest dramatically, then winced as the movement pulled his burned arm. "Heart racing like... like..."

"Like you almost got arrested?" I checked the corridor. Empty for now. "Why are you really here? And don't say grandmother."

His bravado crumbled. He looked at the floor, at the walls, anywhere but at me. His jaw worked, trying to form words that wouldn't come.

"I..." He swallowed hard. Tried again. "I need to get to Zamami. Is very important."

"Important enough to steal a uniform and risk prison?"
