He nodded. Didn't elaborate.

I watched him. Read the microexpressions: fear, desperation, guilt, determination. Fifteen years old and completely out of his depth. But not lying about the stakes. Whatever he was chasing on Zamami mattered more than his own safety.

The burn made sense now. He'd encountered something — someone — enhanced. Thermal operative. Champion class. And he'd survived, barely, with information he didn't know was classified.

Liability. But possibly useful.

"Take the uniform off," I said. "You're about as covert as a fireworks display."

"But how will I—"

"Not my problem. Figure it out or get arrested. Your call."

I left him there, wrestling with oversized sleeves and the weight of very bad decisions.

\---

Knees protested as I climbed the stairs to the open deck — that grinding sensation, bone on bone, cartilage that had given up pretending to do its job. I made a note: \*L. knee instability, 1353-1407 hrs. Manageable. Suboptimal.\*

They'd read it. They always did. Monitoring my decline with the same clinical interest they'd apply to any deteriorating asset. How many more missions before she's too broken to deploy? Place your bets, gentlemen.

I dry-swallowed two pills from the blister pack in my jacket. Not the good stuff — that was rationed, saved for when I couldn't afford to limp. These were just NSAIDs, over-the-counter garbage that barely touched the inflammation. But they kept me functional.

Functional was all the job required.

The deck air bit cold despite the afternoon sun. Salt spray misted across the railings, leaving everything slick and treacherous. I found a spot near the stern where I could watch both entrances and settled in to wait.

The water looked worse up here. That dead-blood darkness stretched to the horizon, broken only by the ferry's wake — a white scar that healed behind us as fast as we made it. In the distance, Zamami was a gray smudge against grayer sky, getting closer with the slow inevitability of a deadline.

Beautiful, in a broken-glass kind of way. Like watching something die in slow motion and calling it sunset.

Bonta emerged fifteen minutes later, stripped down to a threadbare shirt with a faded Gundam print and jeans that had seen better decades. The uniform was bundled under his good arm like evidence he didn't know how to dispose of.

He spotted me. Hesitated. Then approached with the guilty shuffle of a kid who knew he was about to get yelled at.

"What do I do with this?" He held up the uniform, helpless.

I nodded at the water. "Ocean's right there."

His eyes widened. "But—but littering is—"

"\*Ocean.\*"

He hesitated, then flung it overboard with the conviction of someone committing their first real crime. We both watched it sink — olive drab swallowed by dark water, gone in seconds.

"So," I said, not looking at him. "What's actually worth risking prison for?"

"My grandmother." Immediate. Practiced. "She's very sick. I need to see her before—" He made a vague gesture that probably meant \*death\* but looked more like \*confused bird.\*

I let the lie hang there. Didn't bother puncturing it.

Movement to my left. Two soldiers — different ones, fresh rotation — approached with the casual swagger of men who'd been given vague orders to "look official." The lead one was stocky, crew-cut, with the kind of face that looked angry even when smiling.

"Papers," he barked. Not asking.

I handed them over. Again. He scanned them with less care than Scar had, clearly going through motions.

Then his eyes landed on Bonta.

"Who's the kid?"

I didn't hesitate. "My assistant."

Bonta's head whipped toward me, eyes wide. I stepped on his foot. Hard. He swallowed whatever protest had been forming.

Crew-cut looked between us. Skeptical. "Assistant for wild boar assessment?"

"Field work requires local knowledge. He's from Zamami. Knows the terrain." I kept my voice flat. Bored. Like I'd had to explain this a hundred times already. "It's in the manifest."

It wasn't. But the manifest was forty pages of bureaucratic tedium and Crew-cut didn't look like the reading type.

He grunted. "Kid doesn't look like much."

"He's cheap," I said. "Budget constraints."

Bonta made a small offended noise. I stepped on his foot again.

Crew-cut handed the papers back, already losing interest. "Stay out of the way."  
They moved on. Bonta waited until they were out of earshot, then: "Assistant? Assistant?"  
"You're welcome."  
"But I don't know anything about boars\!"  
"Neither do I, kid. But now we're both committed to the lie." I pulled out my phone, opened the notes app. Started typing. "Stay close. Don't talk to anyone. And if you see those soldiers again, you're carrying my equipment and taking field notes for minimum wage."  
"How much is minimum?"  
"About a thousand yen an hour. Maybe less if you keep asking stupid questions."  
His face fell. "That's... that's terrible pay."  
"File a complaint with HR."  
"You don't have HR\!"  
"Exactly. Welcome to contractor work."  
My phone buzzed. One long pulse. Encrypted call, scrambled routing. I knew the number.  
"Stay here," I told Bonta. "Don't move. Don't talk. Don't even breathe loud."  
"But—"  
"Don't."  
I walked to the bow, where engine noise and spray would cover my voice. Answered on the third ring.  
"Yeah?"  
"Status." His voice — clipped, familiar, carrying the weight of shared history neither of us wanted to discuss. No greeting. Never a greeting.  
"On schedule. Kadena Marina to Zamami, ETA thirty-five minutes."  
"Complications?"  
I glanced back. Bonta was exactly where I'd left him, leaning against the railing and trying to look invisible. Kid was better at following orders than expected.  
"None worth reporting."  
Pause. The kind that meant he didn't believe me but couldn't prove it. Yet.  
"You still grind your teeth when you're lying?"  
"Wouldn't know. Haven't lied to you recently."  
"Cute." Exhale — cigarette smoke, I could hear it through the line. "The brothers are moving faster than expected. Shade's already on Zamami. Scorch is sick, possibly compromised. Sun's off-grid."  
"Define compromised."  
"Fever. Aggression. Exposure symptoms. If he's turning, you'll need to contain him before he becomes a liability."  
Turning. Such a clean word for what the nodes did to people. Like saying someone "caught a cold" when they were coughing blood.  
"And if containment isn't an option?"  
"Then terminate. You know the parameters." His voice hardened. "The Janus gene is priority one. Everything else is secondary."  
"Including the brothers?"  
"Especially the brothers. They're carriers, not assets. If any of them become unstable—"  
"Neutralize. I know the drill."  
"Do you?" The question hung there, loaded. "Because last time you had a choice between mission success and collateral preservation, you chose wrong."  
My jaw clenched. Old wound, still tender. "That was different."  
"Was it?" He didn't wait for an answer. "Champion operative is confirmed active on Zamami. Thermal class. You'll recognize the signature."  
The kid's burn flashed through my mind. Textured. Coral-pattern. Fresh.  
"Noted," I said. Kept my voice neutral. "Anything else?"  
"Watch yourself. And Koi?" His tone shifted. Almost concerned. Almost. "How's the knee?"  
"Functional."  
"That's not what I asked."  
"It's what you're getting." I ended the call before he could push further. Small victories.  
Note for file: Civilian asset (Bonta Higa, age \~15) encountered Champion operative prior to ferry boarding. Thermal contact burn consistent with C-Class augmentation, fresh trauma indicating recent engagement. Possible witness to Champion activity. Potential intel value: moderate. Threat assessment: minimal. Currently traveling under false pretense (grandmother cover story \- obvious fabrication). True motivation unclear but appears personal/desperate in nature.  
Improvised cover: assistant contractor. Soldies accepted explanation without significant scrutiny. Asset now operationally linked to K-97 for duration of transit. Will maintain cover until Zamami arrival, then reassess utility.  
Secondary note: Would never actually pay the kid more than 1000 yen/hour. Maybe less if he keeps asking stupid questions. Maybe nothing if he keeps staring at my tits like a concussed pigeon.  
Tertiary note: Left knee degradation continues. Logged instability episodes at 1353, 1407, 1438 hours. NSAIDs maintaining baseline functionality. Condition suboptimal but mission-capable. Request medical supply requisition upon return to mainland.  
When I returned to the stern, Bonta was humming — some J-pop song I half-recognized, mangled by his off-key enthusiasm. He stopped when he saw my face.  
"Everything okay, onee-san?"  
"Fine." I leaned against the railing, let the spray mist my face. "Just checking in with people who pay me."  
"Are they nice peoples?"  
"No."  
"Oh." He went quiet. Then: "Are you nice people?"  
I looked at him. Really looked. Fifteen years old, burned arm hidden under a thin jacket, chasing something on Zamami that scared him more than soldiers and prison. Desperate enough to risk everything. Stupid enough to think lying would work.  
"No," I said. "I'm not."  
He nodded slowly. Like that confirmed something he'd already suspected.  
The loudspeaker crackled.  
This time, the sound was wrong from the first syllable. Pitched too high, warbling like a record played at the wrong speed.  
The captain's voice: "Attention passengers we are approaching—"  
Cut. Static hiss.  
Then: children singing.  
High and sweet, a nursery rhyme I didn't recognize. Japanese, but the dialect was old. Pre-war old. The kind that survived in folk songs and forgotten textbooks.  
"Red shoes, red shoes, the girl wore red shoes—"  
Bonta's humming stopped. His head cocked, listening. Confused.  
"She went away on a foreign ship—"  
The children's voices fractured. Multiplied. Now there were dozens, layered and echoing like they were singing from inside a cave. Or a mass grave.  
"She went away and never came back—"  
The melody twisted. Minor key bleeding into dissonance. Wrong notes held too long.  
"What... what is that?" Bonta whispered.  
I didn't answer. Couldn't. Because the children's voices were fading, replaced by something worse.  
Military bark. Sharp commands in Mandarin, clipped and precise: "All units to stations. Perimeter check. Possible contact on approach vector—"  
My training. I recognized the drill call. Naval intercept protocol, standard PLA. But the voice was wrong — too many speakers layered together, consonants duplicating and echoing like the audio had been copied and pasted until it lost coherence.  
Then: my own voice.  
Metallic. Hollow. Echoing across the ferry with the flatness of old surveillance footage:  
"...survive by playing roles. Masks inside masks. The question is which one bleeds when the knife goes in."  
I'd said that. Four months ago. Debriefing after the Ishigaki extraction, when they'd pressed me about operational methodology and I'd been too tired to lie properly. Secure room. Encrypted recording. Meant for classified archives buried so deep even I'd need clearance to access them.  
How the fuck was it playing on a civilian ferry?  
The loudspeaker died. Real silence this time — not just absence of sound, but the thick pressure of something listening. Waiting. The air itself felt heavier, like atmospheric pressure dropping before a storm.  
Bonta's face had gone pale. Salt spray dripped off his lashes, or maybe those were tears. He was fifteen. This was too much. All of it.  
"That wasn't you talking," he said. Quiet. Almost hopeful. Voice cracking on the last word. "Was it?"  
I kept my eyes on the horizon — dark water, darker sky, the thin line between them blurring as Zamami grew closer. Almost there. Almost done with this particular nightmare.  
"Didn't say a word," I told him.  
The ferry's engines churned. The water swallowed the sound. And somewhere in the static-laced silence, I could still hear children singing about red shoes and foreign ships and girls who went away and never came back.  
Just like Bonta's sister, I thought. Then filed the thought away with all the other operational data I'd collected:  
The burn. The lie. The desperate eyes. The way he kept glancing toward Zamami like it held something precious he'd lost.  
Kid was useful. Potentially.  
If I could keep him alive long enough to matter.  
\[Report filed 1847 hours, Zamami approach\]  
Status: Mission-capable. On schedule. No significant delays.  
Assets: Civilian witness acquired. Potential intel value pending further assessment.  
Threats: Champion operative confirmed active. Audio anomalies require investigation — possible psyops, signal intrusion, or equipment compromise. Recommend communications sweep upon landfall.  
Personal: Knee holding. Pain managed. Still functional.  
Conclusion: Proceeding to primary objective. Will maintain cover and monitor for further anomalies. Civilian asset designated temporary contractor status for operational security. Will reassess utility upon arrival.  
\[End log\]

## [Zamami Infiltration \- Village of Voices](https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/1Hl0Kn-tIJ3CySIv9Cg1Yt2XDlXyuch07Te9NJElBR4s/edit)

**FIELD OPERATION LOG: K-97**  
 **Location:** Zamami Village, Okinawa Prefecture  
 **Date:** \[REDACTED\]  
 **Time:** 16:47 Hours  
 **Mission Objective:** Locate American asset, codename \[████████\], last contact with Subject S. Haven (Target: Eldest)  
 **Secondary:** Track Subject K. Haven (Target: Middle / "Shade")  
 **Status:** IN PROGRESS  
*\[Note: The following document was recovered from corrupted storage media. Sections marked \[REDACTED\] indicate data loss, interference, or post-mission sanitization. Marginalia in second hand remains unidentified. —Archivist\]*
