**16:47 \- VILLAGE PERIMETER**  
Rain clouds pressed down on Zamami Village like a held breath. The afternoon had that pre-storm weight—air thick enough to chew, Pacific wind dying to nothing. My knee complained as I crouched behind a weathered concrete wall, but the NSAIDs were keeping it functional. Barely.  
Objective markers painted themselves across my field of vision courtesy of the Glassigs: three patrol routes highlighted in amber, the izakaya SANTA (三楽) pulsing red two blocks northeast. Intel suggested the American was being held there. Intel also suggested Sun had made contact seventy-two hours prior, which meant whatever the hostage knew, it was worth keeping alive.  
For now.  
"Onee-san, do you see—"  
"Don't." I didn't look at him. Kept my eyes on the patrol. "Call me Timeera. Just Timeera."  
"Hai, sorry, I mean—yes, Timeera-san."  
Close enough.  
Bonta Higa crouched beside me, all fifteen-year-old elbows and nervous energy. His burned arm was wrapped in bandages that were already coming loose. Kid couldn't even dress a wound properly. I made a note: *Civilian asset requires constant supervision. Tactical liability.*  
But he could translate. And crawl through spaces I couldn't anymore.  
That made him useful.  
"What do you see?" I asked.  
He squinted past the wall. "Three guards, PLA. Sharing cigarette. Very casual, like... like they're not worried about anything."  
"They aren't." I adjusted the Glassig lens, zooming in. The guards' faces were slack, eyes unfocused even as they laughed at some joke I couldn't hear. "Because they're not really guarding."  
"What you mean?"  
I tapped my ear. "Listen."  
The guards' laughter carried across the empty street—but underneath it, something else. A second rhythm. Like an echo that came *before* the sound.  
Bonta's face went pale. "That's... is that...?"  
"Voice layering. Parasite symptom." I logged the timestamp. **16:51 \- Confirmed puppet activity, three units.** "Stay close. Don't make noise."  
"But I need to—"  
"Later."  
I moved.  
---

**16:53 \- MARKET SQUARE APPROACH**  
*\[Handwritten margin note: "Why didn't she abort when civilian contamination was confirmed?" \- Unknown reviewer\]*  
The market square should have been empty. Occupation protocol, curfew enforcement, pick your excuse. But it wasn't empty. Two dozen people milled through the stalls—shopping, talking, going through the motions of normal life.  
Except nothing about it was normal.  
I stopped at the corner, pulled Bonta down beside me. "Watch."  
An elderly woman at a fruit stand picked up a mikan, examined it, set it down. Picked up the same mikan. Examined it. Set it down. The loop repeated. Exact same movements, same head tilt, same pause.  
"Gadzooks," Bonta whispered. "She's like... like a machine."  
"Puppet," I corrected. "The parasite doesn't replace cognition completely. It hijacks motor function, vocal patterns. They're still conscious. Just not in control."  
The fruit vendor—a man in his forties with a sweat-stained apron—turned toward us. His mouth moved, but the voice that came out was wrong. Too deep. Too precise.  
"Civilian presence restricted to approved zones. Violators will be detained for processing."  
Military cadence. Command structure. Coming from a fruit vendor.  
I logged it: **16:54 \- Voice appropriation confirmed. Command authority transplanted into civilian host.**  
"That's not his voice," Bonta said. "That's... that's like a soldier. A officer."  
"Commander, probably." I watched three children near the fountain. They stood in formation—perfect spacing, parade rest—and sang in unison:  
*"Freedom is entrapment,*  
 *Entrapment is freedom,*  
 *The voice that speaks for you*  
 *Is the only voice you—"*  
\[REDACTED\]  
*\[Margin note: "Censored by whose authority?" \- Unknown\]*  
The singing cut off mid-word. All three children turned toward us simultaneously. Bonta grabbed my arm.  
"We gotta go, we gotta—"  
"*Hey nigga, you gonna paint a picture? Get yo ass over here and get low\!*"  
The voice came from a woman sweeping the steps of a closed pharmacy. But it wasn't her voice. It was young, male, American.  
Scorch.  
I felt my chest tighten. Logged it anyway: **16:56 \- Subject L. Haven voice confirmed in civilian host. Parasitic harvest active.**  
Bonta was shaking. "That's... that's from the battle. I heard them say that. When they were fighting. How is she...?"  
"Because the island doesn't just record voices," I said, keeping my own voice flat, clinical. "The voices record the island."  
*\[Handwritten margin note: "Antimetabole or actual operational theory?" \- Unknown\]*  
I pulled him into the shadow of an alley. The Glassigs painted new routes—one patrol approaching from the west, another circling back from the harbor. We had maybe three minutes before the net tightened.  
"**OBJECTIVE UPDATE: Locate izakaya key. Source: \[CORRUPTED\] ...suggest checking... \[STATIC\] ...old woman, age... \[DATA LOSS\]**"  
The codec crackled in my ear. My handler's voice, breaking apart like wet paper.  
"Say again," I whispered into the throat mic. "You're cutting out."  
"...key holder... \[REDACTED\] ...village elder... Kanemura-san... \[INTERFERENCE\] ...consigliere to the... \[CONNECTION LOST\]"  
Gone.  
I pulled the earpiece out, examined it. No damage. The interference was environmental. Or something worse.  
"Timeera-san, what we do now?"  
I looked at him. Fifteen years old, burned arm, sister kidnapped, adventure dream turning into nightmare fuel. He was trying so hard to keep the archaic exclamations going—"gadzooks" and "merciful heavens"—like if he performed the role, reality would match it.  
I should have sent him back to the ferry.  
"We find the key," I said. "There's an old woman—Kanemura-san. My contact says she's the consigliere around here. The one people actually listen to."  
"Oh\! Oh, I know her\!" Bonta's face lit up. "She's friend of my family. Very old. Like, execrably old."  
"How old?"  
"117 years\!"  
That couldn't be right. "You sure?"  
"Yes, yes\! She live through Battle of Okinawa, American occupation, everything. Everyone know her. She like living history."  
Living history. Or something wearing its skin.  
But if she had the key, I needed her.  
"Where?"  
"Near the harbor. But Timeera-san, we have to be careful. She... she know things. See things. My grandmother say she has the—" He paused, searching for the word. "—the sight. You know?"  
"I know," I said. "Move."  
---

**17:12 \- HARBOR APPROACH**  
*\[17:12:44 \- Timestamp loop detected\]*  
 *\[17:12:44 \- Previous entry detected\]*  
 *\[17:12:46 \- ERROR: CHRONOLOGICAL SEQUENCE COMPROMISED\]*  
The shadows were wrong.  
Not just dim—*wrong*. Too dark for late afternoon, edges too sharp, angles that didn't match the sun's position. I looked up. The sky had dimmed by at least three stops in the last five minutes, like something was filtering the light.  
"Is there eclipse today?" Bonta whispered.  
"No."  
But the darkness spread anyway. Not clouds. Something else. Something moving between us and the sun.  
I didn't look up again.  
We moved through the harbor district using cargo containers for cover. The Glassigs painted patrol routes, but twice the markers glitched—guard positions jumping thirty meters in a single frame. Either the system was corrupted, or the guards were moving in ways that didn't follow normal human locomotion.  
I logged it as equipment malfunction. Easier than the alternative.  
"**CONVERSATION INTERCEPT 1 \- 17:15:22**"  
Two soldiers stood near a shipping crate, voices low. The Glassigs auto-translated from Mandarin:  
**SOLDIER 1:** "...heard from Beijing. They're calling it a threshold test. Not just Okinawa. Taiwan, Philippines, even—" \[REDACTED\]  
**SOLDIER 2:** "And we're what? Lab rats?"  
**SOLDIER 1:** "We're proof of concept. Seven nodes, seven different environments. Whoever runs all seven, they get the \[DATA CORRUPTED\] ...entire Pacific becomes..."  
**SOLDIER 2:** "That's insane."  
**SOLDIER 1:** "That's geopolitics."  
They moved on before I could hear more. I saved the recording, flagged it for analysis. *Seven nodes.* That matched the intel fragments I'd been collecting. But proof of what concept?  
The file auto-corrupted three seconds after I saved it.  
**\[ERROR: FILE INTEGRITY COMPROMISED \- RESTORE Y/N?\]**  
I tried. Got static.  
---

"**CONVERSATION INTERCEPT 2 \- 17:16:03**"  
Movement to our left. A puppet civilian—elderly man, sun-worn face—swept the dock in methodical strokes. Back and forth. Back and forth. His broom never varied rhythm.  
Then he spoke.  
The voice that came out was high-pitched, child-like, completely wrong for his weathered frame:  
"*Mama says to clean room before dinner, but sister never clean her side—*"  
He didn't pause. Didn't stop sweeping. A second voice emerged from the same throat, deeper, paternal, overlapping the first:  
"*—room is a shared space, requiring equitable maintenance distribution according to household protocols—*"  
Then a third voice, mechanical, translated simultaneously in Mandarin:  
"*清洁是每个人的责任—*"  
All three voices at once. The broom kept moving. Same rhythm. The man's eyes never blinked.  
Bonta made a sound—half laugh, half strangled cough. His hand flew to his mouth. "That's... that's deeply weird. Like, profoundly weird."  
I grabbed his shoulder, squeezed once. *Quiet.*  
The sweeping man's head turned toward us. Slow. Too slow. Like something was calculating the angle.  
All three voices spoke in perfect unison:  
"*Compliance ensures survival. Survival ensures compliance.*"  
Then he returned to sweeping. The same patch of dock. Over and over.  
I logged it: **17:16 \- Multi-voice manifestation. Simultaneous translation capacity. Philosophical loop suggests higher-order contamination.**  
"Catalog it," I whispered. "Move on."  
Bonta nodded, but his face had gone pale. "They're still in there, aren't they? The people. They're still awake inside."  
I didn't answer. Couldn't. Because he was right, and that made it so much worse.  
---

"Timeera-san, look."  
Bonta pointed at the pier. A group of civilians—maybe twenty—stood in a circle around a single woman. She wore a floral yukata, face lined with a century of sun and salt. They weren't praying. They were just... standing. Waiting.  
"That's Kanemura-san," Bonta whispered.  
I zoomed in with the Glassigs. The old woman's lips moved, but I couldn't hear what she was saying. The civilians around her swayed slightly, like reeds in wind that wasn't blowing.  
"We need to get closer," I said.  
"But the soldiers—"  
"Will be handled. You stay here."  
"But—"  
"Bonta." I looked at him. "This is reconnaissance, not a social call."  
*\[Subtext: Stop being a kid. This is how you get killed.\]*  
He flinched, but nodded.  
I moved alone.  
---

**17:23 \- KANEMURA CONTACT**  
The old woman's eyes tracked me before I'd cleared half the distance. Sharp eyes. Too sharp for 117 years.  
The civilians around her didn't react as I approached—just kept swaying, kept breathing in that too-synchronized rhythm that made my skin crawl. I stopped three meters out, kept my hands visible.  
"Kanemura-san," I said in Japanese. "I need information."  
She smiled. Teeth surprisingly intact. "Information. Everyone needs information these days. But information needs you back, Koi-san."  
I didn't ask how she knew my name.  
"The American being held at the izakaya. How do I get in?"  
"Through the door, naturally." Her smile widened. "But which door? The one that guards see, or the one that guards don't?"  
"I need the key."  
"Ah. The key." She reached into her yukata, pulled out a tarnished brass key on a loop of cord. Held it up so it caught what little light remained. "This one opens the back entrance. Service door. Very quiet. Very discreet."  
She didn't hand it over.  
"What do you want?" I asked.  
"Want? Oh, I'm too old for wants, dear. But I have questions. So many questions." She tilted her head, bird-like. "The boy with you. Higa-kun. He's looking for his sister, yes?"  
My jaw tightened. "That's his business."  
"And yours, now that you've brought him along. Funny thing about voices, Koi-san. They carry. Even when we don't want them to." She leaned forward. "Especially when we don't want them to."  
The civilians around her spoke in unison, a chorus of overlapping tones:  
"*The voice we silence*  
 *Is the silence that voices*  
 *Our—*"  
\[REDACTED\]  
They stopped. Dead air. Then resumed swaying.  
I logged it: **17:26 \- Mass puppet coordination. Chorus pattern. Philosophical content suggests ideological sublayer.**  
*\[Margin note: "This section shows signs of memetic contamination. Recommend full psychological screening for reviewing personnel." \- Unknown\]*  
"The key," I said.  
Kanemura-san handed it over. The cord was warm, like it had been against skin for a long time.  
"One more thing," she said. "The American you seek—he's not a prisoner. He's a guest. There's a difference."  
"What difference?"  
"Prisoners want to leave." She paused, studying my face. "The analyst who mapped Node 3\. Your people want him back very badly. The question is whether he wants to go. He's been talking to your eldest brother, you see. Learning things. Sharing things. Dangerous things."  
My pulse kicked up. Node 3\. That was Iheya—the hive assimilation node. If this analyst had mapping data...  
"What did he tell Sun?"  
"Ask him yourself. If you can get him to stop listening to the island long enough to speak."  
She turned away, and the civilians parted to let her through. The circle dissolved, people wandering off in different directions like the show was over.  
I was about to leave when I heard it.  
Humming.  
Low, distant, coming from the direction of the village center. The same melody from the brothers' helmet cam footage—the tune that had played while Sun worked his tablet, while the shadows moved wrong, while everything fractured.  
*Thrummm-hummm-thrummm.*  
I looked back at Kanemura-san.  
She was gone.  
---

**17:34 \- HARBOR DISTRICT \- SEPARATION INCIDENT**  
"Bonta." I kept my voice low, scanning the alley where I'd left him. "Bonta, we need to move."  
No response.  
"Kid, this isn't funny." I checked the Glassigs—no heat signature where he should be. "Bonta."  
Nothing.  
My heart rate spiked. The handler would kill me if I lost the civilian asset. Worse, *I'd* kill me. Kid was fifteen, burned, traumatized, and I'd left him alone in a puppet-infected village.  
I whispered harder: "Bonta, answer me."  
Silence.  
Not just from him—from everything. The harbor ambience had dropped out entirely. No waves, no wind, no distant engines. Just my own breathing and the blood in my ears.  
*The voice I send goes unheard,*  
 *But the unheard voice sends me.*  
*\[Handwritten margin: "She's losing it." \- Unknown\]*  
I moved through the alleys, checking every shadow, every doorway. The Glassigs glitched twice—false positives, phantom heat signatures that vanished when I looked directly at them.  
Then I heard him.  
"...please, I just need to know if you've seen her. Little girl, nine years old, last seen at Araha Beach. Her name is Meio, she..."  
I followed the voice around a corner.  
Bonta stood in the middle of the alley. Alone. Facing a blank wall.  
No house. No door. No structure of any kind.  
Just weathered concrete and a faded advertisement for fish market hours, peeling and sun-bleached.  
He was talking to nothing.  
"Bonta," I hissed.  
He didn't turn. His lips kept moving.  
"She had pink sandals, and her favorite shirt with the turtle on it, and she always carries this little—"  
"*Bonta.*"  
Still nothing. But his burned arm—the one wrapped in loose bandages—hung differently now. The wrapping was tight. Professional. The kind of field dressing I would have done.  
I hadn't touched his arm since the ferry.  
I crossed the distance in four steps, grabbed his shoulder hard enough to hurt. He gasped and spun around, eyes unfocused, pupils blown wide.  
"T-Timeera-san\! When did you—I didn't hear you coming."  
"There's no door," I said slowly. "Bonta. There's no door."  
He blinked. Looked past me at the blank wall. His face went slack with confusion.  
"But... but I was just... she was right there. Kanemura-san's great-great-granddaughter. She answered. We talked. I *remember* talking."  
"Your arm." I grabbed his wrist, lifted it. The bandages were tight, clean, expertly wrapped. "Who did this?"  
"I... what?" He looked down at his arm like he'd never seen it before. "I don't... you did this. On the ferry. Didn't you?"  
"I didn't touch your arm."  
"Then who...?" His voice cracked. "Timeera-san, I don't understand. She was here. The door was open. I heard her voice. She told me..." He trailed off. "She told me something. Something important. I can't..."  
His free hand went to his pocket. Pulled out a small omamori charm—the kind sold at shrines for protection. Faded blue fabric, white cord. It hadn't been there before.  
"Where did you get that?" I asked.  
"I don't know." He stared at it. "I don't know. I don't remember. But it's mine. I know it's mine."  
It wasn't his. I'd cataloged everything in his pockets during the ferry search.  
I logged it: **17:38 \- Subject experienced impossible contact. Memory alteration confirmed. Physical evidence of intervention: re-dressed wound, new object in possession. No heat signature detected at location. No structure present. URGENT: Possible direct entity contact.**  
*\[Handwritten margin note: "How did it touch him without her seeing? What did it say? Why can't he remember?" \- Unknown\]*  
"We're leaving," I said. My voice came out steadier than I felt. "Stay close this time. Don't leave my sight. Understand?"  
"Hai." His voice was small. Childlike. The gadzookery was gone. "Timeera-san? Am I going crazy?"  
I looked at him. Really looked. Fifteen years old. Sister taken. Home occupied. Now standing in an alley holding a charm he didn't own, wearing bandages he didn't remember getting, after talking to a door that didn't exist.  
"No," I said. "You're not crazy. The island is."  
It was a lie. But sometimes lies were all the comfort you had left to give.  
We moved back toward the harbor. But something had shifted. The afternoon felt later than it should, shadows stretching wrong, that persistent hum underlaying everything.  
And I couldn't stop thinking about what Kanemura-san had said:  
*Voices carry. Even when we don't want them to.*  
What if I'd been calling for Bonta, and he genuinely hadn't heard? What if my voice—the one thing an operative relied on for coordination—wasn't reaching anyone?  
What if I'd already started disappearing?  
*\[Note: K-97's psychological evaluation flagged deteriorating confidence in operational capacity. Recommend monitoring for paranoid ideation.\]*  
---

**17:52 \- PUPPET ENCOUNTER**  
*\[VIDEO LOG FRAGMENT \- CORRUPTED\]*  
We were halfway to the izakaya when the fruit vendor found us.  
Same man from the market square, apron still stained, but his movements were too smooth now. Too purposeful. He rounded the corner like he knew exactly where we'd be.  
"Civilian presence restricted to approved zones," he said. Same military voice, but layered now—three or four speakers at once, slightly out of sync. "Violators will be detained for processing."  
I pushed Bonta behind me. "We're just passing through."  
"Passing through *what*?" The vendor's head tilted at an angle that should have hurt. "The island doesn't let you pass. The island makes you *stay*. Makes you *speak*. Makes you—"  
His jaw opened wider than physics allowed. Something moved in his throat—segmented, glistening, pale.  
I'd seen enough.  
"Back door," I said to Bonta. "Now."  
We ran.  
---

**17:58 \- ALLEY REFUGE / HANDLER CONTACT**  
I pulled Bonta into a covered alley between two shuttered shops, pressed my back against the wall. My chest heaved. Not from the sprint—I'd run longer, harder, in worse conditions.  
It was something else.  
A pressure behind my eyes. Building. Like my skull was too small for my brain, or my brain was too large for my skull, or something inside was *expanding* when it should have been shrinking.  
I closed my eyes. Breathed through my nose. Counted to five.  
The pressure didn't ease.  
"Timeera-san?" Bonta's voice came from somewhere to my left. "Are you okay?"  
"Fine." I opened my eyes. The alley tilted slightly, then corrected. "Just need a second."  
"Your face is very pale. Like, execrably pale. Maybe you should sit—"  
"I'm *fine*." The snap in my voice surprised both of us. I softened it. "I'm fine. Just... dehydration. Heat."  
Except it wasn't heat. The day had cooled significantly. And I'd stayed hydrated.  
My knee throbbed. The headache pulsed. And underneath it all, that familiar feeling—the one I'd learned to recognize over the past six months—that sensation of my own body becoming hostile territory.  
I dry-swallowed two pills from the blister pack. Bonta watched but didn't ask what they were for.  
Smart kid.  
The codec crackled in my ear. I held up a hand—*wait*—and pressed the receiver.  
"K-97."  
"Status check." The handler's voice, clear now, no static. He sounded like he was in the next room instead of however many miles away he actually was.  
"Approaching target location. ETA five minutes."  
"Good. Listen, I've been digging into Zamami's history. Thought you should know what you're walking into."  
I glanced at Bonta. He was watching the alley entrance, playing lookout. Good.  
"I'm listening," I said quietly.  
"Zamami's got a nasty reputation locally. Not just war stuff—older. The fishermen won't go near certain parts of the reef at night. Say voices come up from the water. Voices of the drowned, specifically from 1945\. Battle of Okinawa shit."  
"Folklore."  
"Maybe. But here's where it gets interesting: in 2003, there was a marine biology expedition. Deep reef survey. Team of six went down. Only four came back up."  
"Accident?"  
"Officially? Equipment failure. But the survivors all reported the same thing—they heard voices underwater. Not through their radios. Through the *water*. Speaking in languages they shouldn't have understood."  
The pressure behind my eyes pulsed harder. I leaned my head back against the wall.  
"And you're telling me this because...?"  
"Because what's happening now isn't new to Zamami. The island has a history of taking voices. Storing them. Playing them back wrong." He paused. "The parasite didn't create this phenomenon. It exploited something that was already there."  
I filed that away. Another piece of the puzzle that didn't fit the frame yet.  
"Anything else?"  
"Yeah. The kid with you—Higa Bonta. Fifteen years old, sister kidnapped four days ago at Araha Beach by Subject S. Haven and an unidentified thermal operative. You planning to tell me why you're babysitting?"  
My jaw tightened. "How long have you known?"  
"Since the ferry. Satellite caught the thermal signature of his arm wound. Ran facial recognition. Pulled his school records, family history, dental charts. Want me to keep going?"  
I said nothing.  
"Koi, I don't care if you picked up a stray. But if he compromises the mission—"  
"He won't."  
"If he *does*, you handle it. Understood?"  
The weight of that landed. *Handle it.* Such clean language for such dirty work.  
"Understood," I said.  
"And Koi? About that headache you're not mentioning—"  
I pulled the earpiece out. Ended the call.  
He knew. Of course he knew. They monitored everything—vitals, biometrics, probably my fucking dreams if they could figure out the tech. The headaches, the knee, the pills I was rationing because I knew they'd notice if I requested resupply too soon.  
They knew I was breaking down.  
They were just tracking *how fast*.  
I looked at Bonta. He'd turned back toward me, concern written across his too-young face.  
"We're moving," I said. Pushed off the wall. My knee protested but held. "Stay close."  
"Hai." He hesitated. "Timeera-san, about what you said before—about dehydration. I have water if—"  
"I'm fine."  
I wasn't fine.  
But fine was all I had left to be.  
---

**18:04 \- IZAKAYA SANTA (三楽) \- DECISION POINT**  
The izakaya loomed at the end of a narrow street—two stories, traditional architecture, paper lanterns still lit despite the dying day. Two guards at the front entrance. PLA uniforms, rifles, bored expressions.  
Or at least they would have looked bored if their eyes weren't moving in synchronized patterns.  
Puppets.  
I crouched behind a vendor stall with Bonta, pulled out the brass key Kanemura-san had given me. The Glassigs painted two routes in my vision, overlaying the real world with tactical data:  
**ROUTE A \- FRONT ENTRANCE:**  
 *Estimated guard count: 2 visible, 3-4 probable inside*  
 *Combat probability: 87%*  
 *Stealth success: 12%*  
 *Asset risk (Bonta): EXTREME*  
 *Noise generation: HIGH \- will alert surrounding patrols*  
 *Egress difficulty: SEVERE \- limited escape routes if compromised*  
**ROUTE B \- REAR SERVICE ENTRANCE:**  
 *Estimated guard count: 0-1*  
 *Combat probability: 23%*  
 *Stealth success: 74%*  
 *Asset risk (Bonta): MODERATE*  
 *Unknown factors: \[DATA INSUFFICIENT\] \- key source reliability unverified*  
 *Egress difficulty: MODERATE \- multiple exit vectors*  
I stared at the data, then at the actual building. The guards shifted their patrol pattern—perfectly synchronized, three steps forward, pivot, three steps back. Inhuman precision.  
The humming was louder now. Close. Coming from somewhere inside the izakaya.  
*Thrummm-hummm-thrummm.*  
"Timeera-san?" Bonta whispered. "What we do?"  
I pulled out my field notebook—actual paper, the kind that couldn't be remotely corrupted—and started writing:  
---

**TACTICAL ASSESSMENT \- 18:04 HOURS**  
*To whoever reads this (Archivist, Handler, Board of Inquiry, or just some poor bastard cleaning out my effects):*  
*Here's the situation. I've got two options, both bad in different ways.*  
*OPTION A: Front door. Direct approach. I can probably drop the visible guards before they alert—emphasis on "probably." But combat generates noise, noise brings reinforcements, and reinforcements mean Bonta (civilian asset, age 15, zero combat training, recently traumatized) becomes collateral. If I go loud, I'm betting his life that I'm faster than every puppet in earshot.*  
*The math says I'm not.*  
*OPTION B: Back door. Stealth approach. Quieter, safer for the kid, better egress options. But it requires trusting the key from a 117-year-old woman who may or may not be fully human anymore. It requires trusting that the "service entrance" isn't a trap. It requires trusting on an island where trust itself has been parasitized.*  
*By every tactical metric, Option B is superior. Lower risk, higher success probability, preserves operational security.*  
*But here's what the metrics don't capture:*  
*If the key doesn't work—if Kanemura-san played me—we're trapped at the back of the building with no cover and no fallback. The front entrance at least offers the clarity of immediate threat. You know what you're fighting. You know the cost upfront.*  
*The back door? That's faith. And I've never been good at faith.*  
*So I'm asking you—whoever you are, reading this in whatever future exists after Zamami—what would you do?*  
*If it were your call, your mission, your fifteen-year-old tagalong who's already seen his sister taken and his home occupied, what would you choose?*  
*The known violence of the front door?*  
*Or the unknown risk of the back?*  
*Because I'm standing here with a headache that suggests my brain is eating itself, a knee that might give out if I have to sprint, and a kid who's counting on me to make the right call. And the metrics can calculate probability, but they can't calculate cost.*  
*They can't tell me what it means to get Bonta killed because I chose stealth over certainty.*  
*They can't tell me what it means to compromise the mission because I chose caution over speed.*  
*So here's my decision matrix, laid out for review:*  
---

**FRONT ENTRANCE (ROUTE A):**  
**Pros:**

* Clear threat assessment  
* Direct path to objective  
* No reliance on unverified intelligence  
* Immediate tactical control

**Cons:**

* High probability of civilian asset casualty  
* Mission compromise if combat extends beyond initial engagement  
* Noise discipline impossible to maintain  
* Egress becomes combat scenario

**Psychological factor:** Choosing this route means accepting that Bonta might die. Can I carry that?  
---

**REAR ENTRANCE (ROUTE B):**  
**Pros:**

* Minimal combat exposure  
* Civilian asset survival probability high  
* Maintained operational security  
* Multiple egress options

**Cons:**

* Unverified intelligence source (Kanemura-san)  
* Unknown interior layout  
* Potential entrapment scenario  
* Requires trust in compromised environment

**Psychological factor:** Choosing this route means betting everything on an old woman who spoke in poetry and disappeared into a crowd of puppets. Can I afford that faith?  
---

*I'm writing this decision point into the record because someone needs to know: whatever happens next, I chose it. Not the algorithm. Not the handler. Not the mission parameters.*  
*Me.*  
*And if it goes wrong—when you're reading this in some archive, wondering how K-97 fucked up Zamami—you'll know exactly what I was thinking.*  
*You'll know I saw both doors.*  
*You'll know I understood the stakes.*  
*And you'll know I chose anyway.*  
*So tell me, reader—what would you do?*  
*Which door would you take?*  
*Think carefully. A fifteen-year-old boy's life might depend on your answer.*  
*Mine certainly does.*  
---

I closed the notebook. Looked at Bonta. He was watching the guards, unaware I'd just written his potential death into an official record.  
My knee throbbed. The headache pulsed behind my eyes like something trying to crack through my skull. The pills weren't working anymore.  
But my hands were steady.  
I made my choice.  
"Bonta," I said quietly. "Back door. Stay behind me, stay quiet, and if anything goes wrong—you run. You don't wait for me, you don't look back. You run to the harbor and you find a way off this island. Understand?"  
"But Timeera-san—"  
"*Understand?*"  
He swallowed. Nodded. "Hai."  
I pulled out the brass key. It was still warm.  
"Let's see if faith still works on Zamami," I said.  
We moved toward the back of the izakaya.  
The humming grew louder.  
---

**\[FIELD LOG \- ROUTE SELECTION: REAR ENTRANCE\]**  
**\[CONTINUED IN NEXT DOCUMENT \- IF IT EXISTS\]**  
*\[Final note, handwritten, different hand than previous margin notes:\]*  
*"She chose the back door. The key worked. But what she found inside... that's where the record gets really strange. Where Timeera Koi stops being an operative and starts being something else. Where the questions stop having answers.*  
*I've read this log seventeen times. Every time, I stop at this page and wonder: would I have chosen differently? Would you?*  
*The truth is, there was no right choice. Only the choice she could live with.*  
*Or die with.*  
*We'll find out which in the next file."*  
— *Archivist Ma, Field Recovery Division, 2037*  
---

**END TRANSMISSION**

## [American Analyst](https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/1lJjigVMOG-2qshDlfQFYHRKG-AYT2i9DyQtIDEG-FIA/edit)

**FIELD OPERATION LOG: K-97**  
 **Location:** Izakaya SANTA (三楽), Zamami Village  
 **Date:** \[REDACTED\]  
 **Time:** 16:58 Hours  
 **Mission Status:** Intel Acquisition / COMPROMISED

---

Being in an izakaya before the sun goes down feels like blasphemy. Like showing up to a funeral in a swimsuit—technically you're there, but everything about it is wrong.

The interior was darker than it should have been for late afternoon. Paper screens filtered what little light remained, turning everything sepia-toned and funeral-parlor quiet. But the place was surprisingly clean. Tatami mats swept, low tables arranged in perfect parallel lines, sake bottles lined up on shelves with military precision. Traditional Japanese setup, the kind tourists paid extra for.

Except there were no tourists. No staff. No ambient noise of a working establishment.

Just one man, sitting alone at a corner table like he'd been staged there.

I adjusted the Glassigs, let the facial recognition scan him. The codec crackled softly in my ear—Handler's voice, clipped and efficient:

Target confirmed. Intel package incoming.

The data overlaid my vision in amber text:

**CHANCE, Elliot Caine**  
 **Age:** 29 (Handler noted: "Looks younger—stress or genetics")  
 **Build:** 5'8", 165 lbs, average but not malnourished  
 **Current State:** Dress shirt (white, partially unbuttoned), slacks (charcoal), no jacket. Handler's assessment: "Impeccable despite captivity—vanity or denial mechanism."  
 **Observable Stress Markers:** Dark circles, jaw tension consistent with bruxism, hands unnaturally still

**Tactical Assessment:** No guards outside. No restraints visible. No explanation.

That last part bothered me most. If he was a prisoner, where were the guards? If he wasn't a prisoner, why was he still here?

The silence felt watched. Not empty—*occupied*. Like standing in a room where someone just left but you can feel their body heat still in the air.

I logged it: **16:59 \- Surveillance dread confirmed. Absence of visible security suggests alternative monitoring.**

Bonta shifted beside me, nervous energy radiating off him like heat shimmer. Kid was wound tight—burned arm, missing sister, too many questions he didn't want answers to.

Elliot's eyes tracked us as we approached. Sharp eyes. Alert.

Not charmed. Not relieved. *Suspicious.*

"How did you just get in here?" His voice was smooth—NPR-host smooth—but carried an edge. "I heard commotion outside. Who the hell are you people?"

I kept my expression neutral. Professional. "Diplomatic contractor. Agricultural consultant. Wild boar assessment."

His eyes narrowed. Scanned me head to toe, then Bonta, then back. Calculating.

"Right," he said slowly. "And I'm the Emperor of Japan."

Smart enough to recognize tradecraft when he saw it. That was useful. Or dangerous.

"You're younger than your file suggested," I said, matching his tone. Clinical. Detached.

"You've got a file on me?" A bitter smile. "Flattering. Though I thought they'd send someone. Just didn't think it'd be a model and a kid."

I let that hang for exactly two seconds. Long enough to acknowledge, not long enough to seem affected.

"Flattering," I said. "Now tell me why you're still breathing."

His smile faded. He gestured at the empty izakaya with one hand—a smooth, almost theatrical motion.

"I've been expecting someone. Not you specifically. But someone."

Bonta cleared his throat. "Are you... are you okay? Do you need medical—"

"I'm fine." Elliot's attention shifted to him. "You're Okinawan. Local?"

"Uruma City," Bonta said quickly. Too quickly. "But my family—"

"Doesn't matter." Elliot cut him off, not unkindly. Then back to me: "You want to know why I'm here. Fair question. Simple answer: I don't know."

"Try harder," I said.

He sighed. Leaned back slightly, hands still flat on the table. Performatively casual.

"I was with others initially. Six of us. Researchers, contractors, one journalist. They came for us one by one—guards, soldiers, I couldn't tell the difference. Took them somewhere. Never saw them again."

"How long ago?"

"Couple of days? Time's wrong here. Clocks run but they don't *move*, you know what I mean?" He paused. "I'm the last one left."

Bonta's face went pale. "Where did they take—"

"It's no use." Elliot's voice flattened. Lost the smooth edge. "You're not getting off this island. You're not getting out of Okinawa."

The room seemed to contract. That surveillance feeling intensified—like the walls were leaning in.

"Why are we trapped?" Bonta whispered.

I glanced at him. Kid's voice had gone small, childlike. This was hitting him harder than I'd expected.

Elliot studied us both. Then something shifted in his expression—resignation, maybe, or dark satisfaction at having company in his hell.

"How does it work?" I asked. Kept my voice level. Professional.

He smiled without humor. "You want the technical explanation or the practical one?"

"Both."

"Technical: encrypted air traffic control, naval blockade enforced by joint PLA-JSDF coordination, and land routes that don't exist because we're on an island chain. Practical?" He leaned forward. "Air? Encrypted. Sea? Blockaded. Land? There is no land."

**\[ARCHIVIST'S NOTE \- ATTACHMENT 1\]**  
 *Retrieved from corrupted USSFDOA files: Satellite imagery dated March 1, 2025\. Okinawa Prefecture surrounded by concentric blockade zones (red/amber/yellow). Notations in margin: "No civilian aircraft crossed perimeter after this date. Three attempted. All crashed. No survivors."*

"The nodes," Elliot continued, his voice taking on that circular quality—talking *around* the point before landing on it. "You have to understand, the containment isn't just military. It's technological. Seven different nodes scattered across the island chain. They're all responsible for the blockade. That's all I know. Well—" He glanced at the wall behind him, where a framed photo hung. Generic izakaya decoration: fishermen at sunrise. "—that's all they've *told* me."

Something about the way he looked at that photo made my instincts itch. Too quick. Too deliberate.

I logged it: **17:04 \- Subject exhibits possible stress indicators. Photo fixation noted.**

"Seven nodes," I repeated. "What do they do?"

"I told you—the blockade."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the only one I have." His pupils dilated slightly. Odd. The light hadn't changed. "This one—Node 1, Zamami—it's active. I know that much. The others..." He trailed off. Touched the wall near the photo frame unconsciously. "They're out there."

**\[ARCHIVIST'S NOTE \- ATTACHMENT 2\]**  
 *Recovered schematic: Node installation design. Antenna array forming non-Euclidean geometry. Engineer's notation: "Installation complete 2024-11-07. Activation protocols remain classified. DO NOT APPROACH STRUCTURE DURING OPERATIONAL HOURS."*

Bonta was staring at Elliot with something between pity and horror. "But there has to be a way out. There has to—"

"How are *you* planning on getting out?" I cut in. Not we. *You*. Tactical distance.

Elliot's face twisted—not quite a smile, not quite a grimace. "I've spent fourteen months calculating exit vectors. I was sent here to observe, to report, to redeem." He touched his left breast pocket. Just a brush of fingers, but deliberate. Something was in there. Photo, maybe. Or ID. "Air's encrypted, I told you. Sea's blockaded. And land..." He laughed—a brittle, hollow sound. "I don't have the clearance codes. I don't have the extraction protocols. I don't have anything except this goddamn room and the certainty that I'll be here until the nodes go dark. Or I do."

Silence. Heavy. The kind that made you aware of your own breathing.

"Reassignment," Elliot said quietly. "That's what they called it. Okinawa was supposed to be redemption. I was supposed to prove I could—" He stopped. Jaw clenched. "Doesn't matter. I'm here. You're here. We're all here."

I logged it: **17:08 \- Subject displays depressive indicators. References "redemption" without context. Possible disciplinary reassignment. Singapore incident flagged in file—connection unclear.**

**\[ARCHIVIST'S NOTE \- ATTACHMENT 3\]**  
 *Personnel file excerpt \- E. Chance: "Transferred from Singapore facility following incident 2023-08-14. Casualties: 17 exposed, 12 deceased. Subject Chance cleared of criminal charges but flagged for mandatory psychological evaluation and reassignment pending review. Note: Subject requested Okinawa posting specifically. Motivation unclear."*

"I need information," I said. Clipped. Professional. "American soldier. Black, mid-twenties, military bearing. Seen him?"

Elliot's expression shifted—something like recognition, or maybe relief at a question he could actually answer.

"Around noon today. Maybe couple hours ago." He gestured vaguely north. "Heard he was heading to the Cenotaph for the War Dead Who Wish for World Peace. Old World War II memorial, just outside the village. North side."

I knew the location. Tourist landmark. Quiet place. Good for covert meetings.

Or ambushes.

"You *heard*," I repeated. "You didn't see?"

"I heard guards talking. They don't know I understand Mandarin." He paused. Pupils dilated again—that same odd flicker. "He looked purposeful. Like he knew where he was going."

I had what I needed. Intel acquired. Mission parameters satisfied.

"We got what we need," I said. Stood. Signaled Bonta with a glance. "We're leaving."

"Wait\!" Bonta didn't stand. Stayed seated, looking at Elliot with those too-wide kid eyes. "Timeera-san, wait."

I stopped. Didn't sit back down.

Bonta turned to Elliot. His voice cracked slightly. "Why are they doing this? All of this—the blockade, the nodes, everything. Why?"

Elliot stared at him. Really *looked* at him, like he was seeing Bonta clearly for the first time. A kid. Fifteen years old. Burned arm. Asking questions that had no good answers.

His composure cracked.

Not dramatically. Just a slow fracture—shoulders sagging, hands sliding off the table, that smooth voice going rough.

"I don't know," he said quietly. "I've spent fourteen months here. Observing. Reporting. Trying to understand the pattern. And I don't know enough to get off this island. I don't know enough to do *anything*." He looked down at his hands. "I thought I was smart enough to figure it out. Map it. But the architecture doesn't make sense. The voices don't—"

He stopped. Blinked hard.

"You have to understand," he said. Voice strange now. Flatter. "You have to understand, I tried."

The humming started.

Low. Distant. Like standing near high-tension power lines.

*Thrummm-hummm-thrummm.*

I'd been hearing it as background noise since we entered. But now it was louder. Closer. Coming from everywhere and nowhere.

"We're leaving," I said. Grabbed Bonta's shoulder. "Now."

Elliot smiled. That smooth, bitter smile. "I suppose you are. Give your target my regards. If he's still himself."

We turned toward the door.

Three steps.

The humming intensified—not gradually, but *surging*. Like someone turned a dial from three to eleven.

My tactical instinct screamed: **TRAP.**

I glanced back.

Elliot was still seated. Hands flat on table. Same posture.

But his shadow on the wall behind him was wrong.

Too tall. Shoulders angled differently. Head cocked at an impossible degree.

And the photo on the wall—the fishermen at sunrise—one of their eyes was *open*. I'd seen it when we entered. The figures had been frozen mid-laugh, eyes squinted against dawn light.

Now one fisherman stared directly at me. Unblinking.

Wrong posture. Wrong shadow. Wrong eyes.

"Timeera-san..." Bonta's voice shook.

"Timeera Koi."

Elliot's voice.

But flattened. Stripped of smoothness. Like someone had run his vocal cords through a compressor and removed all human texture.

Multiple tones underneath. Layered. Overlapping.

Static. Children singing. Military cadence. Something *else*.

He stood. Too fluid. Joints bending at angles that didn't hurt.

My hand moved to my weapon. Didn't draw. Not yet.

"You came for information," Elliot said. But it wasn't just his voice anymore. His mouth moved but the sound came from *everywhere*—the walls, the ceiling, the photo frame, the sake bottles vibrating on their shelves.

His voice. Timeera's voice. Bonta's voice. All braided together:

*"Your voice. Their voices. All voices are mine."*

**\[ARCHIVIST'S NOTE \- ATTACHMENT 4\]**  
 *Transcript timestamp corruption detected:*  
 *17:42:33*  
 *17:42:33*  
 *17:42:35 \[ERROR \- CHRONOLOGICAL SEQUENCE IMPOSSIBLE\]*  
 *Audio analysis flagged: 7 distinct vocal signatures detected simultaneously. Identities: \[REDACTED\], \[REDACTED\], Subject L. Haven (Scorch), civilian child (female, age 8-10), PLA Command (Mandarin), ferry captain, and Subject E. Chance.*  
 *Note: Something happened here that the recording equipment couldn't capture properly.*

Elliot's mouth opened.

Not just opened. *Unhinged*. Jaw dislocating with a wet crack, stretching wider than physics allowed.

And then came the scream.

Not a scream. **The** scream.

Every voice on Zamami releasing at once. Every stolen word, every harvested cry, every parasitized breath erupting from his throat in a cacophony that didn't sound human because it *wasn't*.

It was primal scream therapy inverted—not catharsis, but *weaponization*. Every repressed voice finding release through violence.

Timeera's voice from the ferry: *"...survive by playing roles. Masks inside masks..."*

Bonta's voice, younger, terrified: *"Meio\! Meio, where are you?\!"*

Children singing in unison: *"Red shoes, red shoes, the girl wore red shoes—"*

Military commands overlapping: *"All units to stations / 清洁是每个人的责任 / Freedom is entrapment—"*

Scorch's voice, raw and angry: *"Get yo ass over here and get low\!"*

And underneath it all, a bass rumble that vibrated in my chest like standing too close to a jet engine.

Sound as weapon. Sound as *violence*.

The paper screens tore. Windows cracked—not shattered, but spider-webbed with stress fractures. Sake bottles rattled on shelves. One fell. Exploded against tatami.

Bonta screamed. Couldn't hear it over the cacophony. Blood ran from his nose—bright red against pale skin.

I grabbed him. Tackle-dragged him toward the back exit. My ears weren't ringing. They were *shrieking*. Internal damage. Ruptured something.

Elliot—Cipher—whatever the fuck he'd become—didn't pursue.

Just stood there. Mouth impossibly wide. Screaming every voice at once.

We hit the alley. Stumbled. Crashed against the opposite wall. Bonta retching, me gasping, both of us bleeding from ears and nose.

The scream continued. Muffled by walls but still *there*. Inside my head. Echoing in the spaces between thoughts.

Psychological residue. The voices would follow us now. That's how mimicry worked—it planted itself in your memory and *lived* there.

Not captured. Not killed. Not freed.

Just contaminated.

Bonta looked up at me. Face pale, eyes too wide, blood dripping off his chin.

"Was he still in there?" he whispered. "Was Elliot still—"

I didn't answer. Couldn't. Because the truth was worse than either option.

Elliot was still in there. Conscious. Aware.

Watching himself scream with someone else's lungs.

---

**\[FINAL NOTE \- K-97 DIRECT ADDRESS\]**

Whoever's reading this—if you've ever heard a scream like that in person, you'd think you were in a nightmare. The kind where you try to run but your legs don't work, try to scream but no sound comes out.

For me, it wasn't a nightmare.

It was standing right there in that izakaya, watching a man's mouth open too wide, hearing every voice he'd ever stolen pour out at once.

For you, it's just words on a page.

Lucky you.

**\[END LOG \- 17:47 Hours\]**  
 **Status:** MISSION COMPROMISED / INTEL ACQUIRED / CIVILIAN ASSET TRAUMATIZED  
 **Recommendation:** Avoid Node 1 central structures. Cipher confirmed active. Elliot Chance designation updated: PUPPET / CHORUS HOST / LOST

---

**\[ARCHIVIST MA \- RECOVERY NOTE\]**

This was the last coherent transmission from K-97 before she went dark for 36 hours. When contact resumed, her reports showed signs of auditory hallucinations—specifically, hearing her own voice speaking from sources that shouldn't have been capable of speech (radios, trees, corpses).

The scream she described has been corroborated by three separate witnesses in Zamami Village. All report the same phenomenon: a sound that "came from everywhere" and "wouldn't stop even after it stopped."

Two of those witnesses are now non-verbal.

The third keeps asking the same question: "Was he still in there?"

I don't have an answer.

Neither did K-97.

## [Cenotaph for the War Dead Who Wish for World Peace](https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/18izzXdQYTVmbCmOb_FvVaxwXioE7goi02q3sD0C-ieE/edit)

The Cenotaph for the War Dead Who Wish for World Peace sat like a crouched giant at the forest's edge. Concrete and stone, weathered by decades of salt wind and survivor's guilt. The memorial walls curved inward—arms trying to hold something that had already escaped.

Twilight made the kanji inscriptions bleed into shadow.

I stood at the base of the approach path, pack heavy, Glassigs hanging around my neck like a judgment. The forest behind the monument was *dense*—not thick, *dense*—like the trees had decided to form ranks and wait.

Going back to that place of uncertainty always shakes me. A ground as solid as fresh clay, **srrp srrp** go the steps through it.

Intel said Scorch was spotted heading this direction three hours ago. Three hours in Zamami time, which meant anything. Could've been yesterday. Could've been next week. The island didn't respect clocks anymore.

No sign of the brothers in this dense forest.

The uphill climb wasn't an issue. It's just the lack of light that impedes progress. And my decision not to say something to him before they left, that makes me blister. A boot without socks pinching and building pus.

*What would I have said? "Don't go"? "Wait for me"? "I think something's wrong with Sun"?*

Any of those would've been true. None of them would've stopped him.

Scorch did what Scorch did. Sun orchestrated. And I watched. That was the formation. That was always the formation.

*Middle brother. Middle ground. Middle of fucking nowhere trying to hold shit together.*

It's not a good feeling out here, with the forest breathing over my shoulder, like a phantom.

I pulled the Glassigs down over my eyes. **Click-hummmmm**—the startup sequence vibrated against my temples. The world bled away into thermal gradients, everything rendered in shades of orange, blue, and that sickly yellow-green that meant *organic heat*.

The memorial stones registered *warm*. Not sun-warm. Body-warm. Like something had been pressed against them recently. Or was still inside them.

I blinked, adjusting. The Glassigs painted the world in lies and half-truths. Heat where there shouldn't be heat. Cold where warmth should linger.

The trees showed hot-cold-hot patterns that didn't match wind or sun exposure. Vertical streaks of orange running up trunks, then dead blue patches, then orange again. Like something had been *climbing* them. Or like the trees themselves had veins.

I adjusted the sensitivity dial. **Tzzt-tzzt**—static feedback scratched at my eardrums. The display flickered once. Twice. Showed me four heat signatures where there should have been empty forest.

Then three.

Then four again.

"Fucking prototype bullshit," I muttered. But I kept them on. Better than going in blind.

The forest's breath came in wet **shhhhhhh-thicks** beneath the cicada static. Not wind. Something else. Something that moved *with* rhythm instead of random gusts.

I moved up the stone steps, each footfall a careful negotiation. The memorial loomed larger. Through the Glassigs, the kanji inscriptions glowed faint orange—residual heat from the day, or maybe from all the grief that had been pressed into them over seventy years.

*The dead who wish for world peace.*

I wondered what they'd think of us now. Using their island as a battlefield again. Different flags, same blood.

The tree line started twenty meters past the main memorial structure. That's where the path split—one route circling back toward the village, another climbing toward the interior. Toward the places marked on maps with warnings. *Unstable terrain. Do not enter.*

Military talk for *we don't want you to know what's there.*

Through the Glassigs, the forest looked like a fever dream. Trunks pulsed with uneven heat. The canopy showed thermal patterns that resembled neural networks—branching, connecting, passing signals.

I stopped at the tree line. Something moved in there. Heat signature, humanoid, approximately sixty meters in.

My pulse kicked up. I zoomed the Glassigs. **Whrrrrr-click.** The image sharpened.

Six-foot frame. Broad shoulders. The way he shifted his weight—left foot forward, right foot at an angle—that was familiar. That was *muscle memory familiar.*

Scorch.

It had to be.

The signature moved with purpose. Not patrolling. Not wandering. *Searching.* Same thing I was doing.

Looking for each other in the dark like idiots.

I should've called out. Should've used the comms. But the comms had been unreliable since the ferry. Half the time they worked. Half the time they broadcast your voice to everyone in a two-klick radius. And on Zamami, "everyone" included things that shouldn't be listening.

I moved into the trees.

The forest closed around me like a fist. Temperature dropped five degrees in as many steps. The **shhhhhhh-thick** breathing sound got louder. Not coming from one direction. *All* directions.

The trees didn't sway. They *leaned.* Watching.

I've seen a lot of terrain. Urban, desert, jungle. You learn to read spaces. Learn what's hostile and what's indifferent.

This forest was hostile.

The heat signatures through the Glassigs multiplied. Six. Nine. Twelve. All flickering in and out like bad signal. I couldn't tell which were real and which were echoes—thermal ghosts of people who'd been here before.

Or people who were here now but hiding in ways the Glassigs couldn't track.

Fifty meters. The signature I'd marked as Scorch was stationary now. Crouched behind a cluster of banyan roots.

I circled left. Approached from his flank. If it was him, he'd recognize my movement pattern. If it wasn't—

*Why wouldn't it be him?*

*Because nothing here is what it looks like. Because you've been wrong about everything since you landed. Because Sun's voice comes out of walls and Scorch's voice echoes from trees and you're next, you're—*

I shut that down. Negative spiral. Not now.

Forty meters.

The figure shifted. Turned slightly. I caught the profile through the Glassigs—augmented image processing tried to build a face from thermal data, failed, showed me a blur of orange and red where features should be.

But the posture. The way he held his shoulders.

*That's him.*

Thirty meters.

Metal scraped stone somewhere ahead—**skrrritch-tap-tap.** I froze. The figure froze. We were both listening now.

The forest's breathing stopped.

Complete silence. Worse than the **shhhhhhh-thick.** Way worse.

That arrested silence Bonta had written about. Mean silence. The kind that comes before ambush.

Twenty meters.

I could see him clearer now through the thermal haze. He was facing away, rifle slung across his back, one hand touching the tree trunk beside him like he was steadying himself.

Or listening to it.

*Scorch,* I thought. *Turn around. Let me see your face.*

He didn't turn.

Ten meters.

I stepped on something soft. It made a sound like **phut-squelch.** Organic. Wrong.

I looked down. Through the Glassigs, the ground showed hot. Not surface-hot. *Deep*\-hot. Like something underneath was radiating upward.

The figure spun.

Fast. Too fast.

And in that rotation, I saw it. The face through thermal imaging, features sharpening as he turned toward me—

Wrong jaw angle. Wrong height. Wrong everything.

But for one second, before my brain processed it, I *wanted* it to be Scorch so badly that I almost didn't see the rifle coming up.

"Scorch, it's me—"

The figure's mouth opened. No words. Just a low tone, flat and mechanical, like a dial tone held too long.

Not his voice.

I dove left. Rifle report cracked the silence—**CRACK-whip-hissssss**—round passing where my head had been.

Shit. *Shit.*

Another shot. This one clipped the tree beside me, bark exploding into thermal confetti through the Glassigs.

He advanced. Methodical. Professional. Chinese soldier, I could see that now—PLA fatigues, different build entirely. How had I mistaken—

*Because you wanted it to be him.*

He raised the rifle for a third shot. Center mass. Clean kill.

I didn't think. Didn't plan. Just acted.

"Veil Step."

The Dare activated. Energy burned through my system—familiar and terrible. The world snapped into different motion. I wasn't faster. The world was *slower.* Everything stretched and blurred except me.

I crossed the distance in three steps that felt like wading through mud made of time itself. His finger squeezed the trigger in slow-motion. I watched the mechanics—hammer fall, firing pin strike, chamber explosion beginning.

I was already inside his guard.

Palm strike to his wrist. The rifle twisted away, discharged into the canopy—**CRACK-**—birds erupting in thermal streaks of panic.

Elbow to his solar plexus. He folded.

Knee to his face as he came down. Bone crunch. **Crack-crunch-thud.**

The Dare released. Time snapped back to normal speed. The soldier hit the ground hard, unconscious, bleeding from nose and mouth.

Exposure meter in my peripheral vision screamed red. Burning. Burning from one Dare. My hands shook. Vision blurred at the edges.

*Worth it,* I told myself. *Had to.*

*Had to because you fucked up. Had to because you saw what you wanted to see instead of what was there.*

I knelt beside him. Checked pulse—steady. Checked restraints—none. Checked eyes—

They rolled open.

Blood-red. Both irises completely red, like someone had injected dye directly into the capillaries. No pupil dilation. Just flat red staring at nothing.

Cipher's mark. Had to be.

The soldier's mouth moved. That dial-tone sound emerged again, then fractured into static, then resolved into words that weren't his:

"*Looking for your brother? He's looking for you too. Everyone's looking. The island helps us look.*"

The voice layered—his vocal cords, but underneath, something else. Multiple speakers. Overlapping.

I recognized one of them.

*My own voice from three days ago.*

The soldier's eyes rolled back. He went slack. Still breathing, but elsewhere now. Puppet with cut strings.

I stood. Stumbled. Caught myself against a tree trunk.

The bark was *warm* under my palm. Body-temperature warm.

I yanked my hand back.

Through the Glassigs, the tree's thermal signature looked like a heartbeat. **Thump-pulse-thump-pulse.** Orange spreading from core to bark in rhythmic waves.

*Not possible. Trees don't—*

All the trees around me pulsed the same rhythm. Synchronized.

**Thump-pulse-thump-pulse.**

Like they shared one massive cardiovascular system.

I tore the Glassigs off. Needed to see with normal eyes. Needed reality, even if reality was a lie.

The forest looked normal. Just trees. Just shadows. Just the last traces of twilight dying between branches.

But I could still feel it. That pulse. That breathing. That sense of being *watched* by something that had too many eyes to count.

I put the Glassigs back on. Had to. Couldn't navigate blind.

Movement uphill. Multiple signatures. I tracked them—ten, fifteen, maybe twenty figures moving in formation through the forest. Heading northwest. Toward the interior.

Toward Mt. Takatsuki.

I zoomed. The lead figure registered different. Hotter. Not body-heat hot. *Burning* hot. Thermal signature like a forge, radiating out in waves that distorted the air.

Cipher. Had to be.

The thermal operative. The one who'd burned Bonta's arm with a touch.

They were moving with purpose. Military discipline. Heading toward something specific.

*The node,* I realized. *They're going to the node.*

I should've followed. Should've tracked them, gathered intel, reported back.

But I'd just burned a Dare fighting a puppet I'd mistaken for my brother. My Exposure meter was still screaming. My hands still shook. And somewhere in this forest, Scorch was actually *here*, actually searching, actually—

*Actually what? Actually safe? Actually himself?*

*You don't know. You don't know anything.*

The trees rustled. Not wind-rustled. *Purposeful.*

**Shhhhhh-thick-shhhhhh-thick.**

The sound resolved into patterns. Rhythms. Almost like morse code, if morse code could be transmitted through branch-scrape and leaf-whisper.

I listened.

Not with ears. With the part of my brain that had been cataloging wrongness since we landed. The part that tracked patterns even when they shouldn't exist.

The trees were *talking.*

Not language. Not words. But information. Passed from trunk to trunk through that synchronized pulse-rhythm. And somehow—*somehow*—I could parse it.

Images formed in my head. Not visual. Conceptual. Knowledge without source.

*Locals. Underground. Resistance. Planning. Node shutdown. Dangerous. Crazy.*

The word "crazy" came with texture—judgment, dismissal, the way outsiders described people who saw too clearly.

*They're trying to shut down the nodes from inside. Local insurgency. Think they can fix it.*

More rustling. More pulse-patterns.

*Mt. Takatsuki. First node. Cipher goes there. Guards increase. Deadline approaches.*

The information felt *planted.* Like the forest wanted me to know. Or like something *using* the forest wanted me to know.

Manipulation or truth? Both? Neither?

I couldn't tell anymore.

The trees went still. The pulse-rhythm faded. Normal forest sounds crept back—insects, distant birds, the ocean breathing far below.

I was alone with an unconscious soldier with blood-red eyes and a choice that wasn't really a choice.

Find Scorch. Or follow Cipher.

*Find your brother,* instinct said. *That's why you're here.*

*Follow the mission,* training said. *Intel is priority. Cipher's moving on the node. You need to know why.*

*You need to do both,* the middle-brother part of me said. *You need to hold it together. You need to—*

*You need to stop fucking up,* the self-critical voice cut in. *Stop seeing what you want. Stop burning Dares on shadows. Stop being so goddamn useless.*

I looked down at my hands. They were still shaking.

The Exposure meter was fading from red to amber. Another ten minutes and I'd be functional again. Maybe.

If I was lucky.

If luck meant anything on an island that breathed and watched and whispered insurgency through trees.

I made my decision.

Followed Cipher's trail. Northwest. Uphill. Toward Mt. Takatsuki and whatever waited at Node 1\.

Scorch was out here. Somewhere. Searching.

But so was I.

And maybe—*maybe*—we'd find each other at the same destination.

Or maybe I'd just make another mistake. See another shadow that looked like family and burn another Dare trying to save it.

*Middle brother. Middle ground. Middle of fucking nowhere.*

The forest breathed at my back as I climbed.

**Shhhhhhh-thick. Shhhhhhh-thick.**

Watching me go.

---

**END CHAPTER 5**

*\[Archivist's Note: Shade's helmet-cam footage from this encounter shows significant thermal distortion in the final three minutes. The trees' "pulse rhythm" he describes registers on audio as subsonic frequency patterns—too low for human hearing but present nonetheless. Analysis suggests possible infrasound phenomenon, though source remains unidentified. The soldier he engaged was recovered two hours later by PLA patrol. Medical examination revealed total memory loss of the encounter and permanent retinal scarring. When asked what he remembered, he said only: "The trees were counting."\]—Archivist Ma, 2037*

## [\[CIPHER PUPPETS LOG \- ENTRY \#847\]](https://drive.google.com/open?id=13k5gP0VBBV0e1wjsiDa5L6uwg2bxW7QxMCnojm1hAN4)  

\*\*\[Subject: Nakamura Kenji, 72, Zamami Village\]\*\*  

\*\*\[Status: DELIVERED\]\*\*

\*\[Archivist's Note: Extracted from corrupted node data. The cheerfulness disturbs me most. —Ma\]\*

\---

The morning air tastes like mikan peels and salt. Good day for hiking.

I whistle. Don't remember the song. Doesn't matter.

The trail to Mt. Takatsuki winds like—like—

(what was I saying?)

My knees protest but I won't stop. Can't. There's something up there that needs—

What needs?

Fresh air. That's what I told myself. \*Kenji, you need fresh air. Get out of that empty house.\* Empty since Yuriko went away. Forty years of marriage and then.

(don't think about her don't)

There's a thought buzzing since I returned to Okinawa. That strange parturifacient sensation—like something being born inside me, pushing out what used to be there.

\*You're not yourself.\*

Well. Who is? We're all substitutes for our past selves. That's aging, not—

(possession?)

(where did that word come from?)

There's a man I replaced. I know this. Feel it between thoughts. He had my name. My face. My wife.

\*Had\* my wife.

No. I had Yuriko. Forty years. She was mine.

(the way she screamed)

(no she died peacefully)

(didn't she?)

\---

\*\*\[LOG NOTE: Memory conflict detected. Substrate fragmenting. Continue upload.\]\*\*

\---

Halfway up. The questions slide off like water. Better to just \*be\*. Just walk. Just whistle.

"Kenji."

I stop.

The voice comes from behind a boulder. Soft. Familiar. Impossible.

She's there. Yuriko. Twenty-three again. Yellow dress from our first date.

"You're not real," I say. But I'm smiling. Yellowbird smile.

"Does that matter?" She pats the rock. "Come with me. Like we always said."

"I can't. I'm delivering—"

"A message. I know." She tilts her head. "Come with me instead."

My chest aches. "Not yet. Someone needs to know. A soldier. American. Black. His name is—"

The name comes from deep inside. Not my memory. Something else's.

"Shade," I say.

"And what's the message?"

I open my mouth.

Nothing comes out.

"I don't know. I just know I have to."

She stands. Extends her hand. "Then let's go together."

She starts walking. Up. I follow.

Because I always followed her.

\---

\*\*\[LOG NOTE: Subject approaching delivery point. Message payload ready.\]\*\*

\---

The summit is small. A clearing. Rocks. The edge.

The edge drops sharp. Nothing but air, then rocks, then ocean.

I walk to the edge. Feel nothing.

No fear. Just calm. Just purpose.

"Here?" I ask.

Yuriko nods. "The message goes down. You go with it."

"How does that deliver it to Shade?"

"The island will carry it. The island carries everything now."

Perfect sense. The island is alive. The island remembers.

(the island is Cipher)

I look at my hands. Old hands. Forty years of teaching physics. Phase transitions. Potential energy converting to—

"Before we return to the source," I say aloud.

Yuriko smiles. "You always understood."

I look down. The rocks are very far.

"I don't want to die," I say. Casual. Stating fact.

"You're already gone, Kenji. What's walking around—that's not you. That's the substitute."

And suddenly.

It all becomes clear.

\---

\*\*\[LOG NOTE: Terminal clarity achieved. Proceed.\]\*\*

\---

The person I replaced wasn't some stranger.

It was \*me\*.

Real Kenji died six weeks ago. Died when something slipped inside and wore his skin and smiled his smile.

I'm the substitute. The copy. The puppet.

And the message?

\*We are here. We are many. We are patient.\*

\*Tell Shade: the island remembers every voice it takes.\*

\*Tell Shade: his brothers are already ours.\*

\*Tell Shade: he should run.\*

I don't speak it. Don't need to.

The act of jumping \*is\* the message.

"Ready?" Yuriko asks.

I nod. Take her hand.

"It's a good day for this," I say. Cheerful. Bright.

And I step off.

\---

The air rushes up. I don't scream. I'm not afraid.

I'm \*accomplished\*.

The last thing I think:

\*I hope Yuriko forgives me for wearing her husband's face.\*

\---

\*\*\[STATUS: DELIVERED\]\*\*  

\*\*\[Message Integrity: 100%\]\*\*  

\*\*\[Recommendation: Joy is parturifacient.\]\*\*

\*\*\[END LOG\]\*\*

\*\*\[ARCHIVIST MA \- 2037\]\*\*

I've read this forty times. Same horror each time: \*he was happy\*.

Not feigned. Genuine cheerfulness right to impact.

Cipher doesn't create fear. It creates \*contentment\* with your own destruction.

247 similar "deliveries" recovered. Each one casual. Each one leaving a body as message.

Scariest part? We found real Kenji three days later. In his house. Dead for six weeks.

So who delivered the message?

What walked around in Kenji's shape?

And did Shade receive it?

The record suggests yes.

What he did with that warning...

That's for the next file.

## [Boss Battle: Cipher](https://drive.google.com/open?id=1Bo6ig9vlcbKZWePl_o47bvJdKLndhTTqP0KqUero3sk)

\`\`\`

\[AFTER-ACTION REPORT: NODE 1 ENGAGEMENT\]

\[OPERATIVE: K. Haven ("Shade") \- Champion Unit\]

\[LOCATION: Mt. Takatsuki Summit, Zamami Island\]

\[DATE: \[REDACTED\]\]

\[TIMESTAMP: 18:47:33\]
