## [ARCHIVIST FINAL NOTE\*\*](https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/1GSdz49ZarvuUSlovvXLORcwyMKnWMqAWmBGEZWE7jOg/edit)

\`\`\`  
These documents—the lab recording and Dr. TORRES's memo—appear disconnected from combat operations at first glance. Why archive a 1986 laboratory accident in a file system designed for tracking current Okinawa conflict assets?

The answer is simple and horrifying: this is where it all began.

Subject C-1 (Cinder) carries mutation markers identical to the coral specimens from Incident C-447. The Janus gene that powers every DARE Champion, every combat-adapted soldier, every bioweapon deployed in this war—it started with Dr. Zelda TORRES watching a young researcher burn and deciding that burning was beautiful.

One incident. One reckless experiment born from a young man's desperation to prove himself. One mentor's decision to see potential in catastrophe rather than tragedy.

And forty years later, we fight monsters that were born in that lab, carrying that code, expressing that same violent adaptation.

Passion doesn't redeem the harm it causes. Passion compounds. It transforms. It metastasizes.

TORRES is still alive as of this archive date (2026). She's 85 years old, residing in Providence Medical Center, still consulting on DARE program parameters. Still insisting her work serves humanity's future.

Still refusing to admit that her greatest discovery was also her greatest sin.

EJ Whitaker—Cinder—burns through Okinawa Prefecture, leaving ash and ruin, and somewhere TORRES watches the news coverage and sees vindication.

She chose our evolution. We live with the consequences.

—Historical Documentation Unit, DARE Program Archives  
October 2026

## [Scorched Search](https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/1bQMDfMsi1gNDb-Mo9y70HkFcF8vHs8fikzBzq6hOrls/edit)

Shade’s journal

Tokashiki Island at night is like a tomb. In these times, unexpected clatterings and crashing makes it seem like the enemy is always with you in spirit presence. 

Sympathetic and desiring as little trouble as possible from any national military presence, a smaller night-black painted fishing vessel ferried me and other supplies from Furuzamami Beach at around 0011 hrs. The boat bounced through chop as if attempting to shake off all it had seen since the occupation began. Who knows how many wounded or dead it had transported? That choking smell of human humors mixed with attempted sanitation, made it smell like an airport latrine— a fragrant stench lingered. Combined with the constant bobbing, and my flip-flopping gut, there was no chance for sleep.

Good thing was there was no need to conceal myself. The middle-aged Chinese soldier (who seemed to be in-charge) wore a tattered PLA uniform with all insignia removed. I didn’t need to know any Mandarin to realize he had abandoned the fight. When I overheard a woman’s voice soothing a fussing baby behind his rusty cabin door, I understood why.

0318 hrs \- I arrived on Tokashiki. From Tokashiku Beach, its low mountains, silhouetted against the stars, intimidated me. They were the worn and slanted signs outside an vacant and crumbling prison. Me: the Urbex kid with a camera and guts, entering against all good sense. Okinawa at night is oppressive with repressed memory. More so during war.

But still I have to go in. Little brother is there somewhere. Just not sure if he’s the one looking for him, or the one looking for something else. Why did Timeera have to be so vague?

**0331 hrs \- Landing**

The captain cut the engine two hundred meters out. He wouldn't meet my eyes. I understood. Whatever was waiting on that island had a way of making men look away.

I dropped into water too warm, tasted fuel and something brackish underneath. The shoreline came into view as my Glassigs booted up. Not sand. Not anymore.

The burned part of the jungle looked like the island's skin had been broken. A giant's nail had scabbed the earth open—black streaks through the canopy that made it recall napalm fire from the war years. I thought about my grandfather's stories. Except—no, that wasn't right. That wasn't *my* grandfather. That was just the war talking, pressing down on this place like a hand that never let go.

My Glassigs warped immediately. DARE interference. The thermal overlay bled wrong colors, and the audio feed crackled like molten rock cooling, like whispering glass. I toggled off and on twice. Useless. The equipment couldn't parse what happened here.

**0346 hrs \- Into the Burned Zone**

I walked into the tunnel of destruction. A line of burned trees led inland, each trunk a blackened tooth. The path of fire had carved half-pipes of scorched nature, natural waste flushed toward the sea in some old violence I didn't understand yet. But I was starting to feel it.

Every step, the smoldering surface rose up through my boots. Heat that shouldn't still be there. But where the surviving jungle pressed in at the scars' edges, a cooling effect washed over me like someone was watching, or breathing. Both things at once. I kept moving.

The first melted armor was half-buried in char. I recognized it immediately. Scorch-class. Still warm. I collected what I could—three fragments, each one a piece of evidence I didn't want to be collecting. The data upload took thirty seconds. Thirty seconds too long.

Mission note: *He's not controlling it anymore.*

I stared at the words on my visor. Hated writing them. Hated knowing they were true.

**0410 hrs \- Avoiding the Main Road**

The occupation had checkpoints everywhere. I cut inland, away from the coastal routes where patrols moved. The jungle was denser here, less burned, but that didn't feel like mercy. It felt like the island was directing me somewhere specific.

I climbed toward the Kurama Street Observation Deck, staying off the main paths. The elevation gave me sight lines. Gave me distance from whoever was sweeping the lowlands.

**0417 hrs \- The Observation Deck**

The deck overlooked the burned scars—I could see them from up here, dark rivers cutting through the green. The view was meant to be beautiful once. Tourist spot, probably. Now it was just a vantage point where a man could watch what had been done to the island.

I sat and ate the Northern Spy apple. The sweetness had gone bitter, rot underneath the skin like something corrupted from the inside out. I thought about the captain's family—the baby's voice, the woman trying to soothe something in the dark while her man ferried a ghost across Kerama Blue waters. How many people on this island had to leave things behind? How many had to abandon pieces of themselves just to survive?

I spit the apple out.

Around 0441 hrs, I noticed the deck was moving.

Not moving. Vibrating. No—not vibrating. The ground beneath it was *shifting*. Subtle. Almost imperceptible. But I felt it in my bones, in my teeth. A deep, low sound came with it. Not sound exactly. More like the island itself was humming. Breathing. Something massive turning in its sleep beneath the soil.

I forced myself to stay still. To stay quiet. Like moving would wake it.

The Kerama Blue waters glowed faintly in the dark below. The burned scars looked like wounds that wouldn't close. The island kept humming—patient, maternal, endless—and I sat there on an observation deck meant for tourists, realizing I wasn't observing anything anymore.

The island was observing me.

By the time I tried to sleep, the ground was still moving beneath me. Slow. Deliberate. Like something vast was turning over, checking if I was still there.

## Scorch to Shade Communication

Gorillas are docile creatures, but a mask of their head always made for a terrifying Halloween. I was peeling layers of jungle, stalking quiet patrols around, sun trailing over tire tracks, when the face arrived.

It was a meme: shaggy black hair surrounding a rubber primate face with parted rosehip red lips around an air slit, two circular wells for eyes encouraged darkness. The words: "Gorilla Gone" were in white all-caps over the picture. Without candy, orange and black pumpkins, and the *Monster Mash* playing, seeing it there on this dangerous mission was an invasion. 

But I knew who’d sent it immediately.

*Scorch where are you?*

More sentries scan the area. I’m moving closer to the destination. Without thinking as much as I should about circling threats. It’s all sweat, swish, and grit. Occasional crouching and waiting. Charred scars of flaky jungle is the path I follow. Signs of Scorch are everywhere. I contemplate a DARE.

I imagine the conversation between us:

Shade: "Bro, what’s that meme about?"

Scorch: "It’s funny, right?"

"I don’t get it."

"Then don’t get it then. Doesn’t matter."

"It’s the mask."

"What mask?"

"The gorilla mask. Dad’s old one."

"Wasn’t that hard after all, to get it."

"What’s up with you smokin’ everything?"

"All black everything is why."

"You’re not making sense."

"Fumfer. Ha, that’s a funny word."

"What?"

"Phumpher— that’s what you doin’."

"Make sense bro. I ain’t got time for—"

"MUMBLIN’ that’s what it is. Goddamn\! Say it wit’ yo chest\!"

"Say what?"

"What you *really* want to say."

"Which is? You tell me since you already know."

"Because yo ass don’t know loyalty."

"Oh I don’t?"

"All that military shit didn’t give you shit."

"We both went to battle, our own fights."

"Yea but I stayed. You went. And I’m not talkin’ about just Okinawa."

"What is it then?"

"Look at this burnt streak. Look at it nigga\! You think I wanted this??"

"Of course not\!"

"Then hurry yo ass up and get back here."

"Scorch—"

I find myself at a series of friendly buildings. No one is around. But I see the top of a node protruding from the roof. Same construction as the one on Zamami. A trap? There’s no avoiding them here. 

## The Ember Lab

The air reeked of benzene and salt and heat long dead. The walls were stripped bare— steel exposed and oxidized black, consoles gutted and scattered across the floor. Each light that still flickered did so like it was remembering what brightness used to mean.

My GlassIG visor kept shorting. Every step made the deck plating groan, stress fractures spider-webbing under my boots. The deeper I went, the quieter it got—until even my breathing felt like noise.

Then I saw her.

Dr. Hino sat half-folded against a terminal, her legs pinned under a collapsed section of framework. Her skin had gone glassy, like it was trying to imitate what she studied. One amber eye, one gray. The look of someone who had stared too long into a flame.

"Dr. Hino," I said.

She smiled faintly. "You still call me that. I told you once—just Hino. You three never listened."

The words hit like shrapnel. Memories stirred—her voice echoing across sterile halls, calling us "my solar triplets" during drills, sneaking ration sweets to us when the med officers weren't watching. The weight of her hand on my shoulder when a simulation went wrong. *You're learning. You're safe.*

"You remember," she said. "Zelda said you might."

"I remember," I said quietly. "You took care of us."

Her laugh was dry, almost gentle. "Caretaker, handler, mother—whatever word they needed that day. You were all different then. Curious. Hungry for light. Not this…" Her hand twitched, as if trying to outline my silhouette. "Not what you've become."

What I'd become?

I crouched beside her. "Who did this to you?"

She didn't answer right away. Her gaze drifted to the charred impressions burned into her side—finger marks blackened deep into the skin. The burns spiraled, almost deliberate. Almost intimate.

"He did," she said finally. "Your brother. Scorch. But not the one you knew. He burned like he'd lost the idea of himself. *That* wasn't him, Shade. Scorch doesn't *burn* like that—not in his heart. He's good. I know it. Something else was burning through him. The fire was borrowed."

*She still believed in him.* Even now. Even scorched.

"I tried to stop him. He screamed for Zelda, or maybe at her. After that, the soldiers panicked—or they fired—or they prayed. It's all the same. And then they left."

"Left?"

"PLA sealed the node. Let it rot. Cinder stayed behind. Said she'd guard what was left of the Mother's work." Her voice cracked. "But Zelda's still here, Shade. She's at the base."

Her free hand trembled toward my face, then fell. For a moment, she looked like she wanted to reach for me—really reach. Her palm came up and she pawed awkwardly at my head, fingers splaying through my hair like she was checking its weight, its texture. Like she was remembering something.

"You always did have a good grade of hair," she said softly, almost to herself. "Even when you were scared. Even when the tests were hard."

Her hand dropped. Maybe she was afraid the corrosion had made her touch poisonous. Maybe she was just afraid of what I'd become.

"Did Zelda do this to you?" I asked.

"Zelda *is* this," Hino whispered. Her eyes unfocused, staring through the terminal behind me. "She's at the base. The facility breathes *for* her now—or she breathes for it. I can't tell anymore. She's waiting for you, Shade. She's been so patient."

Patience. The word landed wrong.

She reached for my hand, her grip weak but deliberate. "I thought I could shape you all into something better. Stronger. I thought if I loved you enough—studied you enough—science would teach me how to *mother* something the universe never meant to have children. That was my real fire, Shade. Not what Scorch carried. Not what Zelda became." Her laugh was ash-dry, bitter as smoke. "My blindness. My arrogance. I believed I could nurture the inhuman. I believed *I* could be enough."

She released me and gestured toward the floor beside her.

"Use this," she said.

Her fingers brushed a small metallic module—blackened around the ports, faintly humming. The metal was warm to the touch.

"SIG-S," she said. "A GlassIG upgrade. Your visor can handle it. You'll see them now—the frequencies they left behind. The heat they burned into this place. The marks they made when they stopped being human."

I took the module and locked it into my visor. The display shimmered, recalibrating. The world came alive in bands of violet and gold—heat signatures painted across the rubble like ghosts of violence.

And there—heat trails.

Scorch's path scattered and violent through the lab—wild, panicked bursts of thermal chaos. And another, steady and deliberate, moving with purpose through corridors that no longer existed. Precision. Intent.

Past the hollow consoles and twisted framework, deeper into the node's spine.

A circle burned into the far wall. Nine small dots orbiting in perfect synchrony. The solar wheel.

Sun's mark. He'd left his sign in the deep place, like a prayer burned into steel. Like a *claim*. Like a *name*. Astrological. Sacred. Personal.

I touched the symbol. The metal was still warm—or radiant—or warning.

"She's still here," Hino whispered behind me. "She's waiting."

Her voice was fainter now, words skipping like corrupted data. I turned back.

Behind me, Hino's breath rattled once, then stopped. Her amber eye fixed on nothing. Her body went still against the terminal, the weight of it final and absolute.

The terminal at her back flared once and died.

Somewhere beneath the wreckage, the ground quaked—slow, rhythmic, alive. Not random. Deliberate. An earth spasm.

Or waiting.

I followed the tremor toward the node's spine, past the hollow consoles and dead machinery, chasing the pulse that might still be my brother. The solar wheel burned behind me, nine orbiting suns marking the deep places where Sun had been.

The warm metal of the symbol still pressed against my skin like a brand.

Like a covenant.

Like a warning I'd already accepted.

## The Captain’s Conversation

What’s her kink, I wonder?

You think it’s creating life?

Maybe. These days everyone wants to be a mother. Everyone can be. That’s the way it is.

You’re not gonna explain that, are you?

What is there to explain?

Yeah I figured not. Sun?

What’s up?

You have to talk to them about your plan.

You’re right. Creating Lavanyah wasn’t enough for them to get the research. 

Ah her.

She was, or I suppose is, the perfect representation of what can happen when ingenuity meets general soldiering.

Heads up, we’re here. Talk to Zelda?

I don’t want to.

Why not? You followed Cinder for a reason. Watched him burn that kid, collude with the enemy, prolong the war and his overall yuk behavior— don’t tell me all that was for nothing.

Well, when you put it that way…

Hey, I’ve only lived for thousands of years, am the superconstruct of all your past lives, and live rent free in your head. Dozens of wars, whores, millenia of woes, and one learns a thing or two. You’ll see.

Mothers are the murderers of reason. Mothers are the makers of reason. Mothers are rememberers of reason.

Right you are. Okay we’re here. Remember your social skills.

Shut up.

—-  
"You’re looking quite unwell these days, Zelda."

(Cough cough) "Yes, yes I know. The body can never outpace the mind. This is true no matter how advanced we become."

"..."

"Can you come closer? My sight isn’t so good these days… okay that’s better. I’m surprised you came to see me. I hear the fighting is getting worse."

"Only as bad as the politicians want it to be."

"Still railing against the Machine are you? The mob?"

"It’s all the same."

"Depends on the class of mob. Sun, why are you here? You look different."

"I want to know about Project Charon."

## **\[CASE FILE TRANSCRIPT \- SUBJECT: SUN / SUBJECT: ZELDA\]**

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Interior medical facility, Kyoto sector. Subject Sun enters. Zelda is recumbent, life support systems audible. Environmental readings indicate: 22.4°C, medical-grade air filtration, trace pharmaceutical compounds in atmosphere.\]

**SUN:** You're looking quite unwell these days, Zelda.

**ZELDA:** (Cough cough) Yes, yes I know. The body can never outpace the mind. This is true no matter how advanced we become.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Sun remains in doorway. Silhouette. He does not advance immediately.\]

**ZELDA:** Can you come closer? My sight isn't so good these days… okay that's better. I'm surprised you came to see me. I hear the fighting is getting worse.

**SUN:** Only as bad as the politicians want it to be.

**ZELDA:** Still railing against the Machine are you? The mob?

**SUN:** It's all the same.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda's hand moves across the blanket. Tremor detected. Subject's breathing pattern changes—recognition response.\]

**ZELDA:** Depends on the class of mob. Sun, why are you here? You look different.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Sun does not respond immediately. Cardiovascular elevation detected. Perspiration visible on neck/temple region. Subject approaches but does not sit.\]

**SUN:** I want to know about Project Charon.

---

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda's breathing pattern shifts. Pause of 3.2 seconds before response.\]

**ZELDA:** That's a long time ago.

**SUN:** Not long enough. Not if it's still running on this island.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda closes eyes. Opens them. Dual-colored irises (amber/gray) catch ambient light sequentially.\]

**ZELDA:** I conceived of it as a framework. A way to ask: what if human potential wasn't limited by neurology? What if soldiers could think faster, feel deeper, choose better? My disciples heard only the first question. They built weapons.

**SUN:** Weapons that work.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Sun's jaw musculature contracted. Vocal register sharp. Not interrogatory—accusatory.\]

**ZELDA:** Yes. They work. That's what haunts me. They work so well that now everyone wants them. Now I am the architect of necessity.

\[ARCHIVIST NOTE \- Project Charon Genesis: Initiated 2031 as classified framework for cognitive enhancement and somatic optimization. Original parameters emphasized voluntary participation and ethical bifurcation protocols. By 2035, three separate militaries had weaponized the framework independently. By 2039, C-unit designation emerged from Shanghai facilities—first successful integration of Charon protocols into combat personnel. Program was no longer experimental. It was doctrine.\]

**ZELDA:** Come. Sit with me a moment. You're standing like you're ready to leave.

**SUN:** I'm not here to sit with you.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda does not look away. Subject Sun's body language indicates conflict—muscular tension suggesting forward movement, then suppression.\]

**ZELDA:** I know. But I'm old, and my eyes are poor, and I'd like to see my Sun's face while we talk. Is that too much to ask?

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Sun's body shifts toward chair. Muscles flex. Subject catches himself. Turns away. Arms cross. Distance increases. Rejection of maternal reaching.\]

---

**SUN:** How did you lose it? How did it slip away from you?

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Sun's vocal register contains undertone beneath anger. Disappointment detected. Subject's body language suggests need for maternal omniscience—desire for Zelda to have been in control.\]

**ZELDA:** Because I believed that if I explained myself clearly enough, everyone would understand the difference between enhancement and domination. I was naïve.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda coughs. Wet. Old. She continues.\]

**ZELDA:** The vision fractured the moment it left my mind. Shanghai interpreted it one way. Washington another. Now Tokyo. Three nations, three religions, all built from my blueprint.

**SUN:** So you failed.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: No hesitation in Sun's statement. No qualification. Fact delivered as accusation.\]

**ZELDA:** Yes. I failed. And Sun... you're angry with me for failing. But you're angrier with yourself for needing me not to fail.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Subject Sun's jaw tightens. Averts eye contact. Classification: defensive response to emotional exposure.\]

**SUN:** It's not—

**ZELDA:** It's alright. I would be angry too.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Room ambient noise: life support hum. Subject Sun's breathing audible—controlled but at threshold. Military discipline suppressing animal response.\]

---

**SUN:** I need more than what D.A.R.E. gives me. I need the next level. I need what you built into the framework but never released.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda's pupils dilate. Recognition response. Not surprise—acknowledgment. Subject has been anticipating this line of inquiry.\]

**ZELDA:** Ah. So you're already enhanced. They let you in.

**SUN:** Yes.

**ZELDA:** And it wasn't enough.

**SUN:** It's not about enough. It's about what's necessary. There's a war to end, and I—

**ZELDA:** You want to end it alone. You want to carry the world on your back so no one else has to.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Sun turns toward window. Averts direct eye contact. Defensive posture.\]

**SUN:** Don't psychoanalyze me.

**ZELDA:** I'm not analyzing you, Sun. I'm recognizing myself.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda extends hand toward subject. Tremor in extremity. Subject Sun steps backward. Distance increases. Physical rejection of maternal contact.\]

**ZELDA:** That need to solve everything. That belief that if you just become strong enough, smart enough, perfect enough, you can fix what's broken. It destroys you. It will destroy you.

**SUN:** You're not in a position to lecture me about destruction.

**ZELDA:** No. I'm in a position to warn you. There's a difference.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda waits. Extended pause—7.4 seconds. Subject Sun does not respond. Zelda grants spatial and temporal allowance.\]

**ZELDA:** Tell me what you want to know.

---

**SUN:** There's something in the D.A.R.E. framework. Something locked behind genetic thresholds. Bifurcation protocols. I need access. How do I unlock it?

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda's breathing pattern changes. Becomes shallow. Recognition of Sun's true purpose.\]

**ZELDA:** The name they're calling it now is the Janus gene. Named after the two-faced god. One face looks inward. One outward. One is the soldier. One is... you.

**SUN:** How do I activate it?

**ZELDA:** You can't. Not safely.

**SUN:** Zelda—

**ZELDA:** No. Listen to me. When you bifurcate like that, you don't integrate. You become a partition. Two things in one body, each fighting for dominance. The soldier wants to endure. The man wants to rest. The soldier wants victory. The man wants peace. They war inside you. They always will.

**SUN:** I can control it.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda's body stiffens. Flinch response. Not from pain—from inevitability. Subject is hearing her own younger voice speak through Sun's voice.\]

**ZELDA:** No, you can't. Because I couldn't. I bifurcated myself decades ago. I chose the visionary. I let the rest—the mother, the woman, the one who could stop and simply *be*—I let her starve.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Tear fluid detected on Zelda's facial surface. Subject's vocal register indicates emotional clarity rather than weakness.\]

**ZELDA:** I wanted to create soldiers to end wars, but soldiers need wars to justify themselves; wars justify themselves through soldiers—and I knew this. I knew this. And I did it anyway. Don't you see? Don't you see what I'm trying to tell you?

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Subject Sun remains silent. Perspiration visible on temples/anterior regions. Military discipline at critical threshold.\]

**ZELDA:** You're going to make the same choice I did. And look at us, Sun. Look at what I became. Look at what you're becoming. You're going to spend centuries believing it was necessary. You're going to spend centuries being right. And you're going to spend centuries alone.

**SUN:** At least I'm making a choice. At least I'm not pretending it's philosophy.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda recoils as if struck. Subject does not physically advance, but vocal impact registers measurably on Zelda's physiological response.\]

**ZELDA:** You're right. You're more honest than I was. That's something. Come here. Please. Before it's too late. Just... come here.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Subject Sun's body indicates internal conflict. Muscular tension toward forward movement detected. Legs flex. Shoulders shift forward. Internal struggle between maternal pull and defensive armor.\]

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Subject's defensive structure holds. Sun does not advance. Does not move. Distance maintained.\]

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda's arm falls. Her breathing becomes labored—physiological response to emotion and illness intermingling.\]

**ZELDA:** The access codes. They're in the encrypted files under my medical records. Biometric lock. Your DNA should open it. I wanted you to ask. I wanted you to need me for something other than answers.

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Subject Sun enters frozen state. Approximately 2.3 seconds elapse.\]

\[DOOR BREACH DETECTED: Subject Scorch enters. Velocity high. Tactical posture. No hesitation parameters detected.\]

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda's eyes open. Gaze contact with Subject Sun established. Approximately 1.1 seconds of mutual recognition before violence commences.\]

**ZELDA:** Sun—

\[VIOLENCE PARAMETERS INITIATED. Subject Scorch. Target: Zelda. Efficiency rating: maximum. Completion: immediate.\]

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Life support systems cease. Ambient hum terminates. Silence.\]

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Zelda's dual-colored eyes (amber/gray) cease visual processing. Subject is no longer responsive to stimuli.\]

\[CAMERA OBSERVATION: Subject Sun remains in position. Immobile. Access codes mentioned by Zelda constitute informational inheritance now possessed by Sun. Encryption protocols transferable via biometric authorization.\]

---

## **\[ARCHIVIST INTERPRETATION \- FINAL\]**

*Subject Sun's isolation is now compounded. The only figure capable of reaching him—or whom he was capable of reaching—has been terminated. The physical inheritance she offered (access codes, Janus gene activation protocols) has been secured. The emotional inheritance she offered (recognition, warning, motherhood) has been rejected and is now permanently inaccessible. Subject stands in a room with a corpse, holding the keys to his own bifurcation, understanding finally that he will spend centuries alone—not because of circumstance, but because of choice. His choice. Her choice. The only choices available in a world built by people like them.*

*This is the tragedy of enhancement. Not that it fails. But that it succeeds.*

## Post Scorch Attack 

CLASSIFIED DOCUMENT \- DARE PROGRAM HISTORICAL ARCHIVE    
Document Type: Personal Journal Entry (Unauthorized)  
Author: Lt. Yuki Matsumoto, Historical Documentation Unit    
Date: March 23, 2019 \- 03:47 JST    
Location: Personal quarters, Historical Documentation Unit, Kadena AFB  
Security Classification: UNCLASSIFIED (Personal Effects)  
Recovery Note: Found in Lt. Matsumoto's desk after her emergency medical leave (panic attacks, insomnia, acute stress response). Added to official archive per Protocol 7-7-7 (Archivist Mental State Documentation). This entry was written after 47 consecutive viewings of surveillance footage from Sector 4, Camera 19-B.

\---

I can't stop watching it.

The timestamp is burned into my retinas: 23:14:03. Three brothers. One body. One choice that breaks them all.

The surveillance footage is corrupted—thermal interference, electromagnetic distortion from Subject C-3's heat signature. I can see shapes. Movements. Orange fire-glow. But I can't see their faces. Can't see the exact moment Shade's world ends. Can't see Sun's perfectly controlled mask finally crack.

The audio is pristine. Every word. Every breath. Every silence that says more than speech ever could.

So I'm writing it down. Reconstructing what the video won't show me. Using the psych profiles, the family histories, the Janus integration reports. Using the audio to guide me. Using my imagination to fill the gaps.

This isn't protocol. Commander Reyes would have my clearance revoked if he knew. But I can't sleep. Can't eat. Can't stop hearing Lock scream "I was fifteen" over and over.

I need to understand how three brothers who loved each other ended up here.

Here's what I think happened:

The rest of the transcript can't describe the horror of the attack.

A fire so hot I check my skin for burns. Scorch executed conclusively. Zelda's skinned charred then flaked in the vent breeze. Black snow fluttered around the lens. Mouth open, eyes clenched in final agony, she no longer breathed or struggled. Her brilliant mind darkened forever.

For a time, Sun stood there looking at the wall and so did Scorch viewing his kill. I had to fast forward then rewind to the point where they faced each other. Two brothers who rarely (if ever) saw eye to eye on any issue spoke unkind words poised to attack.

"Why?" Sun asked with shakiness in his voice.

"Because she made me like this. I didn't sign up to be some monster\!"

"Perhaps you already were one."

"What\!? Fuck dat supposed to mean\!?" Scorch flared fire from his hands. The eyes were without light.

"You heard me."

"Say somethin’ else then\! Say another godDAMN word."

"..."

"See, see that's what I thought. Got nuthin' to say, cuz you know I'm right. You know all dis shit is cuz of her, right? The C-Unit, being stuck here on this godforsaken island, fuck, prolly even the war itself, all downstream from her fucked up experiment."

Sun stepped forward. "It wasn't your call to make."

"Well, I made it for you, for us."

"No. You did it for your own selfish reasons. Though I have no idea what those are and I don't really give a shit."

Then the screen flashes and Scorch screams.

"Bro\! Bro\! You gotta help me\! It burns\! I'm fuckin’ burnin’ up\! Do something\!"

Sun doesn't move. And that's when the third brother Shade arrives.

\*\*No problem at all\! Here's the complete scene from where you left off:\*\*

Then the screen flashes and Scorch screams.

"Bro\! Bro\! You gotta help me\! It burns\! I'm fuckin' burnin' up\! Do something\!"

Sun doesn't move. And that's when the third brother Shade arrives.

Shade stepped through the smoke like a ghost materializing from thought itself. No sound. No warning. Just there—standing between the body and his brothers, between the killer and the witness.

For a moment, all three of them were still.

Sun stared at Shade with the hollow expression of someone watching a play they'd already memorized. Scorch stared at Shade like he was seeing salvation. And Shade—Shade stared at the blackened shape that used to be Dr. Zelda Torres and felt something ancient and cold settle into his chest.

"Help me," Scorch said again, but this time it wasn't a scream. It was a plea. "Please, Shade. It \*hurts\*."

Shade didn't move. His voice came out flat, empty. "What did you do?"

"What I had to—"

"What. Did. You. \*Do\*."

Scorch's hands ignited, fire curling around his fingers like living things trying to escape. His eyes were wet, wild, jumping between his brothers. "She made us into this\! Into fucking \*monsters\*\! I didn't ask for this shit\! I didn't—" He doubled over, clutching his stomach. Smoke rose from his mouth when he gasped. "It's getting worse. The heat. It's inside me and I can't—I can't make it stop—"

"Good," Sun said quietly.

Scorch's head snapped up. "What?"

"I said \*good\*." Sun took a step forward, and for the first time, Shade saw real emotion crack through that cryptic, distant mask his oldest brother always wore. Rage. Pure and cold and terrible. "You wanted to be a monster, Lock? Congratulations. You're succeeding."

"Fuck you, nigga," Scorch spat. Literally—embers fell from his lips. "You don't get to judge me. You \*chose\* this. You signed up to be their super soldier poster boy. I got \*drafted\* into hell."

"We all got drafted," Shade said, finally finding his voice.

"Bullshit." Scorch wheeled on him, flames crawling up his arms now. "You could've said no. Both of y'all could've said no. But you didn't, because you wanted this. You wanted to be special, to matter, to be more than just—"

"More than what?" Sun's voice was sharp. "More than another fucked-up kid from a fucked-up family? Yeah. I did. And you know what? So did you."

"That ain't—"

"You followed her around like a lost puppy," Sun continued, relentless. "Every time she walked into a room, you'd light up like—" He stopped himself, but too late.

Scorch's flames flared white-hot. "Like \*what\*? Say it."

"Nothing."

"Nah, nah, you started, so finish. Like what?"

Sun's jaw clenched. "Like a kid looking at his mother."

The temperature in the room spiked.

"You don't know shit about—" Scorch started.

"I know she wasn't your mom," Sun said flatly. "I know you made her into something she couldn't be. You needed too much from her."

"And you didn't need \*anything\*," Scorch shot back. "That make you better? Calling her 'Doctor' like she was some stranger instead of the only person who gave a damn whether we lived or died?"

"I respected her position—"

"You were scared," Scorch said. "Scared to get close. But at least you got an excuse—you were already gone before shit got bad. You left first." His eyes slid to Shade. "But \*you\*—"

Shade felt his stomach drop.

"You don't get to say shit to me," Scorch continued, and now his voice was pure venom. "Not after what you did."

"Lock—"

"You were \*there\*," Scorch said, flames climbing higher. "When Dad died. You were home. We both were. And then—"

His voice cracked but he pushed through it.

"And you left anyway."

The words hung in the smoke-filled air.

"Six months," Scorch said quietly. "Dad was gone six months and you couldn't wait to sign up, to get the fuck out, to leave me—"

"I was trying to help—"

"HELP?" Scorch's fire exploded outward. "Nigga, I was \*fifteen\*\! I was fifteen years old and you left me alone in that house with all Dad's shit still in the closets and Mom's pictures still on the walls and you just—you just fucking \*left\*\!"

"I sent money—"

"I didn't need money\!" Scorch screamed. "I needed—" He stopped himself, breathing hard. "I needed you to stay. Just once. Just \*once\* I needed someone to actually fucking stay."

Shade couldn't speak. His throat was closed.

"Sun gets a pass," Scorch continued, looking at their oldest brother. "He was already enlisted when Dad—when it happened. He couldn't come back. I get that. But you?" His eyes fixed on Shade again, wet and red and accusing. "You were there. You helped me pack Dad's stuff. You were at the funeral. You looked me in the eye and said we'd get through it together."

"We did—"

"Then you enlisted three months later," Scorch finished. "Signed up for Janus six months after that. And I was alone. So yeah, when Zelda showed up and gave a damn, when she actually \*stayed\*, I held onto that. And you can judge me for it all you want, but at least I didn't abandon—"

"That's enough," Sun said quietly.

Both brothers looked at him.

"He's right," Sun said, and his voice was flat, empty. "About me leaving first. About—" He looked at Shade. "About you leaving him when he needed you most."

"I was eighteen—" Shade started.

"And he was fifteen," Sun interrupted. "And you were all he had left."

Shade felt something hot and defensive rise in his chest. "What was I supposed to do? Stay in that house? Watch him fall apart? I couldn't—I didn't know how to—"

"So you ran," Scorch said simply. "Like you always do. And then you kept running. Took every field assignment, every deployment, every excuse to stay away—"

"I was doing my job—"

"You were hiding," Sun said quietly.

Shade turned to him. "What?"

"You heard me." Sun's voice was clinical now, distant. "You were hiding from Dad's death. From having to take care of Lock. From having to be the one who stayed. So you volunteered for everything, stayed deployed for months at a time, and when Zelda wanted to run tests, wanted you here for integration—you found reasons to leave."

"That's not—"

"And I wasn't any better," Sun continued, cutting him off. "I was already gone. Already building my career, my reputation. I came back for the funeral and left again two days later because I couldn't—" He stopped, swallowed. "I couldn't handle it either. Couldn't handle seeing Lock try to hold it together. Couldn't handle the empty house. So I left too."

He looked at Scorch.

"We both left you," Sun said quietly. "And you're right. That's on us."

Scorch's flames flickered, died down slightly. For just a moment, he looked like a kid again—young and scared and so desperately alone.

Then his face hardened.

"Doesn't matter now," he said. "Dad's gone. Zelda's gone. We're all that's left and we can't even—"

"You remember Gunstar Heroes?" Shade said suddenly.

Scorch blinked. "What?"

"That old Sega game. We used to play co-op in the barracks. You, me, Sun. Back before—"

"Before everything went to shit?" Scorch finished. "Yeah. I remember."

"You always picked the flame," Shade said softly.

Something flickered across Scorch's face. Not quite grief. Not quite recognition. Something in between.

"We were good at that game," Sun added quietly. "Could beat it with our eyes closed."

"We covered each other," Shade said.

The three of them stood there, and for just a heartbeat, they weren't monsters or soldiers or experiments.

They were just brothers.

Then Scorch laughed, sharp and broken. "Yeah. We \*were\* good. Past tense." He looked at his burning hands. "But look at us now."

The lights went out.

Not flickered. Not dimmed. \*Out\*. Complete and instant darkness except for the fire still consuming Scorch's body, casting everything in hellish orange-red.

Then they heard it.

Footsteps. Heavy. Methodical. Coming from the corridor outside, each impact deliberate, patient, \*wrong\*. The sound of something that was pretending to be human but had forgotten the rhythm.

All three brothers froze.

"Shade," Sun said quietly, his hand going to his weapon. "Tell me you have eyes on—"

"Nothing," Shade whispered. He was scanning, reaching out with whatever senses the Janus gene had given him, trying to feel the shape of the threat. But there was nothing. Just void. "I can't read it. It's like trying to see through smoke."

Scorch's flames dimmed instinctively, reducing to a low burn. For the first time since Shade had arrived, his youngest brother looked genuinely afraid. "No. No no no, he wasn't supposed to be here yet, the intel said he was still in Sector 7, we had \*time\*—"

"Who?" Shade demanded.

The footsteps stopped.

In the silence that followed, they heard breathing. Not human breathing. Too wet. Too deep. Like lungs filled with ocean water trying to remember how air worked.

Then the door exploded inward.

\*\*Cinder\*\* stood in the doorway, and Shade understood immediately why Scorch was afraid.

The thing that had been Dr. Clive "EJ" Whitaker was seven feet of nightmare biology. Coral growths emerged from his skin in branching patterns—not decorative, but structural, like his skeleton was trying to grow outside his body. One eye was human, brown and wet and filled with too much intelligence. The other was a cluster of polyps, each one containing a tiny pupil that moved independently. His face—what was left of it—had been beautiful once. Now it was a fusion of flesh and calcified reef, frozen in an expression that might have been agony or ecstasy or something humans didn't have a word for.

He wore the remains of a tactical vest, but the straps had been modified, cut away to accommodate the coral growth. Weapons hung from him—blades, a sidearm, something that looked like a harpoon gun—but Shade suspected those were just for show. The real weapons were biological.

Cinder's working eye fixed on Scorch.

"You," he said. His voice was a chorus—multiple vocal cords, or maybe the coral itself resonating, creating harmonics that made Shade's teeth ache. "I can smell her on you. Burnt flesh. Calcified bone. The scent signature is identical to—"

His gaze traveled past Scorch. Past Shade. To the body.

Everything stopped.

Cinder stared at Dr. Zelda Torres's corpse with his human eye and his coral eye and all the tiny pupils swimming in polyps, and Shade watched something break inside him that couldn't be put back together.

"No," Cinder whispered. The chorus-voice was gone. It was just him now. Just EJ. "No, she wouldn't—she can't be—"

He took a step forward. Then another. Moving like a man underwater, slow and disbelieving. Scorch backed away immediately, fire surging back to life in panic. Sun raised his weapon but didn't fire, calculating angles, threats, trying to find the tactical answer.

But Shade just watched. Because he recognized what he was seeing.

Grief.

Cinder knelt beside Zelda's body. His armored, coral-encrusted hand reached out—so careful, so gentle—and touched what was left of her face. His fingers came away with ash and char, and he stared at them like they were a language he'd forgotten how to read.

"Thirty-eight years," Cinder said quietly. "I knew her for thirty-eight years. Since 1986\. Since the accident that made me—" He gestured at himself with those ruined hands. "—this."

He looked up at the brothers. All those eyes—human and coral, singular and plural—fixed on them with an intensity that made Shade's skin crawl.

"You think you knew her," Cinder said. "You think because she made you, because she ran your treatments, because she—" His voice caught. "Because she cared about you. You think that means you understood who she was."

"We didn't—" Sun started.

"Let me tell you who she was," Cinder interrupted. The chorus-voice was back now, layered and strange. "To each of you. Since you were too busy arguing about your own pain to see hers."

He looked at Sun first.

"You," Cinder said. "The oldest. The first success. You think she kept you at arm's length because you respected her position?" He laughed, wet and bitter. "She kept you distant because you reminded her of what she could do when everything went \*right\*. You were proof that the theory worked, that Janus integration could create something perfect. And every time she looked at you, she saw her redemption. Her justification for what she did to me."

Sun's face went carefully blank.

"But you couldn't handle being her masterpiece," Cinder continued. "So you called her 'Doctor' and filed reports and kept everything professional because if you let her be your mother, you'd have to admit you \*needed\* her. And you couldn't do that. Couldn't be vulnerable. Couldn't be anything less than the perfect soldier she designed."

He turned to Shade next.

"And you," Cinder said. "The middle child. The one who ran. You think you were hiding from Dad's death, from having to take care of Lock, from the responsibility of being the one who stayed." He shook his head slowly. "You were hiding from \*her\*. From the way she looked at you like you were a problem she couldn't solve. Because you weren't quite as successful as Sun and you weren't quite as broken as Lock, and that made you \*invisible\*. The data point in the middle. The control variable."

Shade felt the words hit like physical blows.

"So you volunteered for field assignments," Cinder continued, relentless. "Stayed away for months. Made yourself \*useful\* instead of present. Because if you weren't here, she couldn't see that you were just... average. Just a soldier who happened to survive the treatment instead of transcend it."

Then Cinder's gaze—all of his gazes—fixed on Scorch.

"And you," he said softly. "Lock. The baby. The one who needed too much."

Scorch's flames flickered but he didn't speak.

"She loved you the most," Cinder said, and the words hung in the air like accusations. "Did you know that? Out of all of you, all the C-Unit subjects, all the experiments and trials and iterations—she loved \*you\* the most. Because you needed her. Because you looked at her like she was salvation instead of science. Because you gave her what she'd been chasing since 1986."

"What?" Scorch's voice was small.

"A second chance," Cinder said. "A chance to be the mother she never got to be. A chance to save someone instead of destroying them." He looked down at his coral-encrusted hands. "She couldn't save me. The accident happened too fast, the integration went too far. By the time she stabilized my condition, I was already—" He stopped. "Already this. But you. You she could still shape. Still guide. Still \*fix\*."

"She didn't fix me," Scorch said bitterly. "She made me worse. She—"

"She made you a \*son\*," Cinder said, and now there was something raw in his voice, something that sounded like envy. "She gave you everything I wanted. Everything I \*needed\*. Attention. Care. Time. She stayed up with you through the fevers. Sang to you when the pain got bad. Held you when you thought you were dying."

He stood now, towering over them all.

"Do you know what she did for me during my integration?" Cinder asked. "She took notes. She measured my vitals. She documented my screaming for the research files. Because I wasn't her son. I was her \*mistake\*. The proof that she needed to be more careful, more precise, more controlled."

"That's not—" Shade started.

"It's exactly true," Cinder said flatly. "For thirty-eight years, I was her guilty conscience. Her cautionary tale. Her reminder that she wasn't God, no matter how much she wanted to be. And I accepted that. I made peace with being the monster in her closet because at least—at \*least\*—she never forgot about me. Never stopped trying to understand what went wrong so she could make it right with the next generation."

He looked at Scorch again.

"And then you came along," Cinder said quietly. "You and Sun and Shade and all the others. The C-Unit. The children she got to keep. The sons she actually wanted." His voice was barely a whisper now. "Do you have any idea what it's like to watch someone love the person you could have been?"

Silence.

Then Scorch spoke, his voice shaking. "If you hated us so much, why didn't you just—"

"I didn't hate you," Cinder interrupted. "I envied you. But I didn't hate you. Because she asked me not to."

He pulled something from his vest—a piece of paper, old and worn, folded and refolded so many times the creases were soft as cloth. He held it up so they could see her handwriting, even if they couldn't read the words from this distance.

"She wrote me letters," Cinder said. "Every month for thirty-eight years. Telling me about her work. About the progress. About—" His voice cracked. "About you. About how Sun was brilliant but distant. How Shade was capable but conflicted. How Lock was struggling but trying so hard to be brave."

He refolded the letter with impossible gentleness and put it back.

"She told me you were family," Cinder continued. "That even though I couldn't be there, even though my condition made me too dangerous for the main facility—you were still my brothers. That we were all her children, even if we came from different failures."

Shade felt something tight in his chest. "EJ—"

"She asked me to protect you," Cinder said, and now the grief was unmistakable, naked and raw. "In her last letter. Three days ago. She said the integration was getting harder to control, that Lock was deteriorating faster than expected, that there was a risk of—" He looked at Scorch. "Of catastrophic failure."

Scorch flinched.

"She asked me to come," Cinder said. "To help. To be ready in case—in case she needed me. And I said no."

The words fell like stones.

"I said no," Cinder repeated. "Because I was tired. Because I'd spent thirty-eight years watching her build a family I wasn't really part of. Because I wanted—just once—for her to need me more than she needed her perfect subjects. So I stayed in Sector 7\. And I let her die alone."

His human eye was streaming tears now. The coral eye leaked that bioluminescent fluid, glowing tracks down his mutated face.

"So when you three stand here arguing about who she was to you—about who failed her, who loved her, who needed her most—remember this:" Cinder's voice dropped to something cold and final. "She was my \*teacher\*. My torturer. My creator. My colleague. My friend. My mother. My punishment. My redemption. She was everything I ever had and everything I ever lost. And I knew her longer than your entire lives combined."

He looked at Zelda's body one last time.

"And I still couldn't save her."

The silence that followed was absolute.

Then Cinder's gaze fixed on Scorch, and something in those multiple eyes changed. Hardened.

"But I can give her justice," he said quietly.

Scorch tried to run.

He made it three steps before Cinder moved.

Cinder's speed was \*wrong\*. Not fast like Sun's enhanced metabolism. Not tactical like Shade's training. It was the speed of a sea creature striking from dark water—alien, impossible to predict, violating every rule about how mass and momentum should work.

He caught Scorch by the ankle. The coral growths on his hand flexed, \*opened\*, revealing structures that looked like sea anemone tendrils. They wrapped around Scorch's leg and Shade heard his brother scream—not from fire this time, but from something else. The coral was \*injecting\* something. Paralytic. Or venom. Or genetic material that would turn flesh into reef given enough time.

Scorch's flames went supernova in panic. The entire corridor lit up white-hot, metal melting, walls buckling. Any normal person would have been atomized.

Cinder held on.

His coral armor smoked and blackened but didn't break. Steam rose from his body, hissing and shrieking, but underneath, Shade could see the tissues \*regenerating\*. Growing back as fast as they burned. Faster.

Cinder pulled Scorch back toward him like reeling in a fish, inexorable, and Shade saw real terror in his youngest brother's eyes.

"Shade\!" Scorch screamed. "Shade, please—SUN—somebody—"

Shade moved without thinking.

He shadow-stepped behind Cinder, blade already drawn, aiming for the gap between coral plates at the base of the skull—

Cinder spun and caught his wrist without looking.

The polyp-eye had seen him. All those tiny pupils, independent, giving Cinder three-hundred-sixty-degree vision. Shade felt something crack in his forearm—not broken, not yet, but close.

"Don't," Cinder said softly. His human eye fixed on Shade now, filled with something that looked like disappointment. "Please don't make me hurt you too. She wouldn't want that."

"Let him go," Shade said through gritted teeth.

"Why? So he can burn his way through the rest of the base? So he can kill everyone else who tries to help him?" Cinder's grip tightened and Shade felt his bones creak. "She \*loved\* him. More than she loved me. More than she loved any of us. And this is how he repays her?"

\*\*BANG.\*\*

Sun's gunshot caught Cinder in the shoulder—not the coral-armored part, but a gap where human flesh still showed through. Blood sprayed. Black and wrong, mixed with something that glowed.

Cinder dropped both Shade and Scorch, staggering back. His head swiveled toward Sun with predatory focus.

Sun ejected the magazine, slapped in a new one without looking, his eyes never leaving Cinder. "Run."

But Scorch was already gone—scrambling to his feet and vanishing down the corridor in a trail of flame and smoke and panic. Running like he'd been doing his whole life.

Shade pushed himself up, coughing. His wrist throbbed but it was already healing, the Janus gene knitting bone and tissue. He looked at Sun, then at Cinder, then made his choice.

He stayed.

Cinder touched his wounded shoulder. Looked at the blood on his fingers. The wound was already closing, coral growing over the bullet hole like living scar tissue.

When he looked back at them, both eyes were dark.

"He runs," Cinder said in that chorus-voice again. "Of course he runs. Coward to the end."

"He's scared," Shade said.

"So am I," Cinder replied. "So was she. Fear doesn't excuse murder."

\*\*CRASH.\*\*

Something exploded through the wall behind them—big, fast, announced by roaring jets and the stink of aviation fuel. A PLA combat mech, eight feet tall, bristling with weapons. It must have been tracking Cinder's biosignature, following the trail of his unique genetic markers.

It opened fire without warning.

Thirty-millimeter autocannon rounds chewed through the corridor. Shade shadow-stepped on instinct, dissolving into darkness as tracers burned through where he'd been standing. Sun moved with enhanced speed, rolling behind cover, already returning fire.

But Cinder—

Cinder didn't dodge.

He turned toward the mech with something that looked almost like \*gratitude\* on his mutilated face, and Shade suddenly understood.

Cinder wanted this. Wanted the fight. Wanted to hurt something the way he was hurting.

The mech's pilot had maybe two seconds to register what was happening before Cinder closed the distance. His movement was wrong—not running, but \*flowing\*, his body moving in ways that violated anatomy and physics. The coral growths on his back extended, became tendrils, became weapons.

They punched through the mech's cockpit glass like tissue paper.

The pilot screamed. The scream ended when Cinder pulled him out—helmet, harness, everything—and threw him into the opposite wall hard enough to turn bone to paste.

Then Cinder tore the mech apart.

Shade had seen a lot of things in this war. He'd seen soldiers do impossible things under stress. He'd seen Janus subjects perform feats that rewrote biology textbooks.

He'd never seen anything like this.

Cinder moved through reinforced titanium like it was cardboard. Each strike pulverized metal, shattered hydraulics, crushed circuitry. The mech tried to respond—spinning up its rotary cannon, deploying smart missiles—but Cinder was already inside its guard, already past its defensive protocols, already \*everywhere\*.

In fifteen seconds, the mech was scrap.

Cinder stood in the wreckage, covered in hydraulic fluid and gore and his own glowing blood, breathing hard. The bioluminescence from his coral growths pulsed in the darkness—blue-green light that made him look like something from the deep ocean, something that had never seen the sun.

He turned back to the brothers.

"More will come," he said flatly. His voice was pure chorus now, all the grief buried under tactical assessment. "This facility is compromised. You need to evacuate."

Shade helped Sun to his feet. His oldest brother had taken shrapnel from the mech's explosion, blood seeping through his tactical vest, but the Janus gene was already working. He'd live.

"What about you?" Shade asked.

Cinder looked at him. Both eyes empty now. Hollow.

"I'm going after Lock," he said simply. "He doesn't get to run from this. He doesn't get to escape consequences." He turned toward the corridor where their youngest brother had fled, following a trail only he could see. "You can try to stop me. But I don't recommend it."

"She wouldn't want this," Sun said quietly.

Cinder stopped. Didn't turn around.

"She's not here to want anything anymore," he said. "Lock made sure of that."

He started walking. Each step deliberate, patient, inevitable.

Shade and Sun looked at each other. No words. They didn't need them. Brothers communicated in the spaces between language, in the things they didn't have to say.

Shade thought about Scorch. About fifteen-year-old Lock, alone in that empty house. About the kid who needed someone to stay and got abandoned instead.

Then he thought about Zelda. About thirty-eight years of letters. About a man turned monster who still called her every month just to hear her voice.

About family— blood and chosen and created— and all the ways it could save you or destroy you or both.

Sun nodded once.

Shade nodded back.

They followed Cinder into the dark.

The hunt had begun.

# Vs. Cinder (Shade’s Account)

Cinder’s first attack was an alarm at 2AM. No time to prepare or defend, just pure reaction speed. I leapt aside. Sun backflipped crazy. 

"You killed her\! The three of you\! I won’t forgive or forget. This flame will deliver burning punishment. Now die\!"

The Glassigs picked up melting heat sigs. Sun fired his rifle, but the molten man was a lunger, and dissolved the rounds.

I took cover in the fast blackening remains of the lab to reach him and Cinder fired hot jets at us. Sweat and soot seemed everywhere.

"We have to Dare\!" I said.

"Agreed\! Now?"

"Now\!"

I held my rifle horizontally. Sun produced his knife. Soon it was sword-length. He swung it hard on my outstretched rifle producing a TANG\! sound that sizzled. Eclipse burst blinded Cinder, then followed up with a shot. For a critical second, his raging fire went campfire, and he cried out.

That opening was all we needed. Sun moved first—always does—while the bustle of Cinder's desperate retaliation filled the lab with impossible heat. The ground under his feet cracked and ran like wax. I pressed forward, muscle memory and something older driving me now, something that had no name but answered to *her*.

*Finish it, Shade— he's breaking, can't hold the form together, the heat's consuming him, look at the seams in his body where the rage is eating through the structure, look look look—*

I fired. Sun's knife came down in that peculiar arc we'd drilled a thousand times. The blade caught Cinder's shoulder. My round caught his chest.

For a moment—and I mean truly a moment, that pocket of time where everything suspends—Cinder stood there, and I saw him. Not the flame. Not the vengeance. Just a man burning from the inside out, finally understanding there was no coming back from this.

Then there was nothing but embers and a sound like glass breaking underwater.

Sun stood there breathing hard, knife still extended. The lab hummed its death-rattle around us.

"Scorch did this," I said. Not a question.

Sun lowered the blade. That was worse than anger. "Yes."

"He's still out there."

"He is." Sun finally looked at me, and his eyes had that quality they get when he's decided something irreversible. "Your brother is redeemable, Shade. I want to believe that. I do." A pause—not hesitation, but finality. "But if I encounter him again, if he comes at me again—I'll kill him. No mercy. No second chances."

My face didn't move. Didn't twitch, didn't show the fissure opening inside me—*something breaking something breaking something that can't be unbroken*—that line we'd never crossed before, that he was crossing now, that I was watching him cross and understanding there was no coming back from it, no coming back from *this*, from him drawing that particular line in ash and blood.

Sun turned. Walked toward the exit without looking back.

"Where are you going?" The words came out before I could stop them.

He paused at the threshold. Didn't turn around.

"To end the war."

I didn't follow. I *knew* not to follow.
